عدم معرفة رواية صحابي عن تابعي لعله غير تام فإن الشيخ جلال الدين السيوطي صنف رسالة الأحاديث المروية من صحابي عن تابعي لكنه قليل جداً لا يقال عليه (و) الدرجة (الثالثة أمر ونهي فالأكثر) قالوا هذه (حجة) لأن الظاهر أن الآمر هو الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم مشافهة وتوقف الإمام لأنه يحتمل الاعتقاد بالأمرية والنهيية (من افعل ولا تفعل، وقد اختلف فيه وقد مر أنهما لهما أم لا فيحتمل فهمه من الصيغتين، فلا يكون حجة على من لا يراهما لهما ورد بأنه بعيد لا يمنع الظهور فإن هذا الاحتمال احتمال الخطأ، وهو بعيد محض، على أنه لا وجه له للتوقف، فإنه يدل لفظ الأمر والنهي على افعل ولا تفعل، فمن زعم أنه للوجوب يعمل به، ومن يزعم أنه للندب يعمل به، ثم التحقيق أن النهي والأمر ليسا إلا الاقتضاء الحتمي، فمعنى أمر ونهى اقتضى الفعل أو الكف حتماً وهذا نقل الحديث الدال على الوجوب والتحريم بالمعنى وهو حجة كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. الدرجة الرابعة : بيان حكم بصيغة المفعول أي بصيغة المجهول كأمرنا وحرم علينا، كما قالت أم عطية : أمرنا أن نخرج في العيدين العواتق وذوات الخدور رواه البخاري. وعنها: نهينا عن عندهم فإن سنة الخلفاء حجة عندهم أيضاً والنزاع في أن لفظ السنة في إطلاق الصحابة لأي سنة فعندنا المتبادر منها طريقة مسلوكة في الدين سواء كانت طريقة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أو طريقة الخلفاء الراشدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم لنا أن السنة لغة الطريقة، ثم عرفا الطريقة الحسنة، ثم طريان النقل لم يثبت بل خلاف الأصل فيبقى إطلاقهم على العرف العام، ويؤيده قول أمير المؤمنين علي رضي الله عنه أفضل الناس أبو بكر ، ثم عمر ثم عثمان (فرفع إلى الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (بلا توقف) فيه (هذا).
Issue: For the wording of the Companion in narration there are seven degrees. First: 'He said to us', 'He informed me', 'He narrated to us', and similar – these are proof without dispute because these words are apparent in direct hearing unless there is a contrary indicator. Second: 'He said upon him' and peace – it is taken as hearing because the apparent state of the Companion is that he only attributes the statement to [the Prophet] through hearing. Al-Baqillani said it is not taken as hearing but also admits the possibility of irsal. Third: 'He commanded and forbade' – most say it is proof because the apparent is that the commander is the Prophet orally. There is dispute regarding the implication of command and prohibition. Fourth: Statement of a ruling in passive form like 'we were commanded' and 'it was forbidden to us'. Fifth: 'From the Sunnah' – it is proof for most because apparent in his Sunnah; for Hanafis it includes the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Sixth: 'From the Prophet' using 'an' – majority consider it as hearing; but the correct view is that it is only attribution, not direct hearing unless based on the question of 'adalah. Seventh: 'We used to do' and similar – apparent in reporting consensus; if it includes 'in his time' or 'he hears', then it is raised to the Prophet without pause.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the seven degrees of wording used by the Companions when transmitting hadith. It is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. The author explains each degree and its probative value (hujjiyyah) with scholarly disagreements.

First degree: Words like 'qala lana' (he said to us) are universally accepted as indicating direct hearing because they are explicit in that sense, unless there is an indicator otherwise. The author cites examples from Hasan al-Basri and the story of the Dajjal to illustrate that even if direct meeting is questionable, the wording still implies hearing.

Second degree: 'Qala 'alayhi' (he said upon him) – most hold it implies hearing because the Companion would not attribute a statement to the Prophet without hearing it. However, al-Baqillani argues it could be mursal (from a subordinate), so its acceptance depends on the principle that all Companions are 'udul (just). The author refutes the claim that Companions never narrated from tabi'un by mentioning Ka'b al-Ahbar and the treatise of al-Suyuti.

Third degree: 'Amara wa naha' (he commanded and forbade) – the majority consider it proof because it is apparent that the command comes from the Prophet directly. Some, like Imam Abu Hanifa, hesitated because these forms might imply recommendation or prohibition without legal force. The author argues that the probability of error is remote and does not negate the obvious meaning.

Fourth degree: Using passive verbs like 'umirna' (we were commanded) – this is weaker than the previous degrees because it could be interpreted as command from a ruler, the Book, or qiyas. For example, the hadith of Umm 'Atiyya 'we were commanded' was understood by the Hanafis as indicating something less than obligation.

Fifth degree: 'Min al-sunnah' (from the Sunnah) – for most it is proof because it clearly refers to the Prophet's way. The Hanafis extend it to include the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs based on the linguistic and customary usage of 'sunnah' as a good path, supported by the hadith of 'Ali about lashing punishments.

Sixth degree: 'An al-nabi' (from the Prophet) using the particle 'an – Ibn al-Salah and a group take it as direct hearing based on common usage. However, the author sides with the majority of usul scholars who say 'an only indicates attribution (mansub ilayh), not necessarily oral hearing. Still, it is accepted as evidence due to the presumption of 'adalah.

Seventh degree: 'Kunna naf'alu' (we used to do) – this is apparent in reporting a consensus of the Companions. Some argue it is not proof because it only describes their action, not a command. But if the phrase is accompanied by a temporal indicator like 'fi 'ahdihi' (in his time) or 'wa huwa yasma'u' (and he hears), then it is raised to the Prophet and becomes definitive evidence without pause. The author illustrates with the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and Abu Huraira regarding the preference for Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthman.

Key Terms
من السنة (min al-sunnah): Fifth degree: Companion says 'this is from the sunnah'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the unique Hanafi position regarding the fifth degree ('min al-sunnah')?
A) It is not accepted as proof.
B) It includes the sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.
C) It only refers to the Prophet's sunnah.
D) It is weaker than the fourth degree.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis accept 'min al-sunnah' as proof that encompasses both Prophetic and Caliphal practices.

Al-Sunnah as Source

Sixth Degree: 'An al-Nabi (from the Prophet using 'an')
Sixth degree of Companion wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dispute regarding the sixth degree and apply the Hanafi position that it is accepted based on 'adalah.

Core Idea
The phrase 'an al-nabi' indicates attribution but not necessarily direct hearing; Hanafis accept it due to the Companions' integrity.

When a Companion says 'an al-nabi' (from the Prophet), Ibn al-Salah and a group take it as direct hearing. However, the majority of usul scholars say it only indicates that the report is attributed to the Prophet, not that the Companion heard it directly. The Hanafis side with the majority but still accept it as proof because of the principle that all Companions are 'udul and likely heard directly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sixth degree wording
Recognize the preposition 'an' followed by 'al-nabi' as the sixth degree.
2
Understand the two views
Ibn al-Salah and some take it as direct hearing (samā') because it is the apparent state. The majority (including Hanafis) say it only indicates attribution (nisbah), not necessarily hearing.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
Despite the ambiguity, the Hanafis accept it as proof because the Companion is 'adl and the default is that he heard it directly unless there is evidence to the contrary.
This is based on the issue of ta'dil.

اتباع الجنائز (والخلاف فيه أقوى من السابقة للزيادة بانضمام احتمال كون الحاكم بعض الأئمة أو الكتاب أو القياس الظاهر إسقاط الكتاب لأنه لا ينافي الحجية، قيل: لا يحتمل الخلاف في أمير المؤمنين الصديق فإنه لم يكن إمام فوقه حتى يأمره وفيه أن احتمال القياس باق. الدرجة الخامسة من السنة) وهو (حجة عند الأكثر للظهور في سنته عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) وعند الحنفية تعم سنة الخلفاء الراشدين لكنه حجة عندهم فإن سنة الخلفاء حجة عندهم أيضاً والنزاع في أن لفظ السنة في إطلاق الصحابة لأي سنة فعندنا المتبادر منها طريقة مسلوكة في الدين سواء كانت طريقة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أو طريقة الخلفاء الراشدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم لنا أن السنة لغة الطريقة، ثم عرفا الطريقة الحسنة، ثم طريان النقل لم يثبت بل خلاف الأصل فيبقى إطلاقهم على العرف العام، ويؤيده قول أمير المؤمنين علي رضي الله عنه وعن آله الكرام جلد النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أربعين وأبو بكر أربعين وعمر ثمانين وكل سنة رواه مسلم، الدرجة (السادسة : عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله أصحابه وسلم ، فابن الصلاح وجماعة حملوه على السماع إذ هو الظاهر من حال الصحابي والأكثر) من أهل الأصول (على احتمال الإرسال) يعني أن السماع بواسطة محتمل وليس بظن سماع بلا واسطة وهو الحق، لأن كلمة عن على أنه مروي عنه ومنسوب إليه وأما أنه مسموع منه فأمر زائد لا يحتمله اللفظ، فإثباته من غير دليل، لكن يكون حجة بناء على مسألة التعديل . الدرجة (السابعة) قول الصحابي (كنا نفعل ونحوه) وهو (ظاهر في نقل الاجماع فالمعنى كنا جماعة الصحابة نفعل جميعاً (وقيل: ليس بحجة لأنه ليس واحداً من الثلاثة لأنه إنما يدل على أن فعلهم كذا لا أنه تعالى أو الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ولا إجماع أيضاً (وإلا كان المخالفة) إياه (خرقاً للإجماع) فيكون الخلاف خطأ، وهو باطل بالإجماع، فإنه لا يخطىء مخالفة (والجواب أن ذلك أي بطلان خرق الإجماع (في) الإجماع (القطعي) وهذا الإجماع ظني، فلا يكون المخالف مبطلاً ( وأما ) قوله : كنا نفعل بزيادة نحو في عهده أو هو يسمع) نحو قول ابن عمر: كنا نتخير في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فخيرنا أبا بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان، رواه البخاري وقول أبي هريرة كنا نقول ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم حي : أفضل الناس أبو بكر ، ثم عمر ثم عثمان (فرفع إلى الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (بلا توقف) فيه (هذا).
Issue: For the wording of the Companion in narration there are seven degrees. First: 'He said to us', 'He informed me', 'He narrated to us', and similar – these are proof without dispute because these words are apparent in direct hearing unless there is a contrary indicator. Second: 'He said upon him' and peace – it is taken as hearing because the apparent state of the Companion is that he only attributes the statement to [the Prophet] through hearing. Al-Baqillani said it is not taken as hearing but also admits the possibility of irsal. Third: 'He commanded and forbade' – most say it is proof because the apparent is that the commander is the Prophet orally. There is dispute regarding the implication of command and prohibition. Fourth: Statement of a ruling in passive form like 'we were commanded' and 'it was forbidden to us'. Fifth: 'From the Sunnah' – it is proof for most because apparent in his Sunnah; for Hanafis it includes the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Sixth: 'From the Prophet' using 'an' – majority consider it as hearing; but the correct view is that it is only attribution, not direct hearing unless based on the question of 'adalah. Seventh: 'We used to do' and similar – apparent in reporting consensus; if it includes 'in his time' or 'he hears', then it is raised to the Prophet without pause.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the seven degrees of wording used by the Companions when transmitting hadith. It is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. The author explains each degree and its probative value (hujjiyyah) with scholarly disagreements.

First degree: Words like 'qala lana' (he said to us) are universally accepted as indicating direct hearing because they are explicit in that sense, unless there is an indicator otherwise. The author cites examples from Hasan al-Basri and the story of the Dajjal to illustrate that even if direct meeting is questionable, the wording still implies hearing.

Second degree: 'Qala 'alayhi' (he said upon him) – most hold it implies hearing because the Companion would not attribute a statement to the Prophet without hearing it. However, al-Baqillani argues it could be mursal (from a subordinate), so its acceptance depends on the principle that all Companions are 'udul (just). The author refutes the claim that Companions never narrated from tabi'un by mentioning Ka'b al-Ahbar and the treatise of al-Suyuti.

Third degree: 'Amara wa naha' (he commanded and forbade) – the majority consider it proof because it is apparent that the command comes from the Prophet directly. Some, like Imam Abu Hanifa, hesitated because these forms might imply recommendation or prohibition without legal force. The author argues that the probability of error is remote and does not negate the obvious meaning.

Fourth degree: Using passive verbs like 'umirna' (we were commanded) – this is weaker than the previous degrees because it could be interpreted as command from a ruler, the Book, or qiyas. For example, the hadith of Umm 'Atiyya 'we were commanded' was understood by the Hanafis as indicating something less than obligation.

Fifth degree: 'Min al-sunnah' (from the Sunnah) – for most it is proof because it clearly refers to the Prophet's way. The Hanafis extend it to include the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs based on the linguistic and customary usage of 'sunnah' as a good path, supported by the hadith of 'Ali about lashing punishments.

Sixth degree: 'An al-nabi' (from the Prophet) using the particle 'an – Ibn al-Salah and a group take it as direct hearing based on common usage. However, the author sides with the majority of usul scholars who say 'an only indicates attribution (mansub ilayh), not necessarily oral hearing. Still, it is accepted as evidence due to the presumption of 'adalah.

Seventh degree: 'Kunna naf'alu' (we used to do) – this is apparent in reporting a consensus of the Companions. Some argue it is not proof because it only describes their action, not a command. But if the phrase is accompanied by a temporal indicator like 'fi 'ahdihi' (in his time) or 'wa huwa yasma'u' (and he hears), then it is raised to the Prophet and becomes definitive evidence without pause. The author illustrates with the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and Abu Huraira regarding the preference for Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthman.

Key Terms
عن النبي (an al-nabi): Sixth degree: Companion attributes a report to the Prophet using the preposition 'an'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of usul scholars, the sixth degree 'an al-nabi' indicates:
A) direct hearing from the Prophet.
B) only that the report is attributed to the Prophet.
C) that the Companion saw the Prophet.
D) that the report is weak.

Key Takeaway
The sixth degree is ambiguous but accepted in Hanafi usul due to the reliability of the Companions.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
7 min
Seventh Degree: 'Kunna Naf'alu' (We Used to Do)
Seventh degree of Companion wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the seventh degree and distinguish when it indicates actual consensus and when it directly reflects the Prophet's practice.

Core Idea
'Kunna naf'alu' may imply consensus, but if tied to the Prophet's time, it becomes direct evidence of his approval.

When a Companion says 'we used to do' (kunnā naf'alu), it is apparent that he is reporting a consensus of the Companions. However, some argue it is not proof because it does not explicitly attribute it to the Prophet or to a divine command. The Hanafis accept it as a report of consensus, which is zanni. If the phrase includes 'fi 'ahdihi' (in his time) or 'wa huwa yasma'u' (and he hears), then it becomes a definitive report that the Prophet approved, and it is accepted without pause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seventh degree
Recognize phrases like 'kunnā naf'alu' (we used to do) and similar.
2
Understand the default ruling
The default is that it indicates the practice of the Companions, which may be considered a consensus (ijma') of the Sahaba.
Consensus of the Companions is a proof.
3
Note the objection
Some say it is not proof because it does not mention a command from Allah or the Prophet, nor is it an explicit consensus.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafis accept it as a report of consensus, but the consensus is zanni (probable), not qati (certain). So opposing it does not invalidate one's faith.
5
Recognize the stronger form
If the phrase includes a temporal indicator like 'fi 'ahdihi' or 'wa huwa yasma'u', then it is considered a direct report of the Prophet's practice (raf' to the Prophet) and is accepted without hesitation.
Examples: hadith of Ibn 'Umar and Abu Huraira.

Source Text

فلتكن هذه الشهادة من هذا القبيل (و) الدرجة الثانية : قال عليه) وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، فيحمل على السماع فإن ظاهر حال الصحابي إنه إنما جزم بنسبة القول إليه بالسماع، لأن الكلام فيمن طالت صحبته وقال القاضي) أبو بكر الباقلاني لا يحمل على السماع، بل يحتمل الإرسال أيضاً، فيبتني قبوله على مسألة التعديل) وهي ظهور عدالة الصحابة وذلك لأنه لم يعرف رواية الصحابي عن تابعي إلا كعب الأحبار) فإنه كان الصحابي الناسب إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم إما سمع من فيه الشريف أو سمع من صحابي والصحابة كلهم عدول، فيكون الخبر حجة قطعاً، وإما من في قلبه من الصحابة شيء فيحتاج إلى التعديل، ثم استقراء عدم معرفة رواية صحابي عن تابعي لعله غير تام فإن الشيخ جلال الدين السيوطي صنف رسالة الأحاديث المروية من صحابي عن تابعي لكنه قليل جداً لا يقال عليه (و) الدرجة (الثالثة أمر ونهي فالأكثر) قالوا هذه (حجة) لأن الظاهر أن الآمر هو الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم مشافهة وتوقف الإمام لأنه يحتمل الاعتقاد بالأمرية والنهيية (من افعل ولا تفعل، وقد اختلف فيه وقد مر أنهما لهما أم لا فيحتمل فهمه من الصيغتين، فلا يكون حجة على من لا يراهما لهما ورد بأنه بعيد لا يمنع الظهور فإن هذا الاحتمال احتمال الخطأ، وهو بعيد محض، على أنه لا وجه له للتوقف، فإنه يدل لفظ الأمر والنهي على افعل ولا تفعل، فمن زعم أنه للوجوب يعمل به، ومن يزعم أنه للندب يعمل به، ثم التحقيق أن النهي والأمر ليسا إلا الاقتضاء الحتمي، فمعنى أمر ونهى اقتضى الفعل أو الكف حتماً وهذا نقل الحديث الدال على الوجوب والتحريم بالمعنى وهو حجة كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. الدرجة الرابعة : بيان حكم بصيغة المفعول أي بصيغة المجهول كأمرنا وحرم علينا، كما قالت أم عطية : أمرنا أن نخرج في العيدين العواتق وذوات الخدور رواه البخاري. وعنها: نهينا عن اتباع الجنائز (والخلاف فيه أقوى من السابقة للزيادة بانضمام احتمال كون الحاكم بعض الأئمة أو الكتاب أو القياس الظاهر إسقاط الكتاب لأنه لا ينافي الحجية، قيل: لا يحتمل الخلاف في أمير المؤمنين الصديق فإنه لم يكن إمام فوقه حتى يأمره وفيه أن احتمال القياس باق. الدرجة الخامسة من السنة) وهو (حجة عند الأكثر للظهور في سنته عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) وعند الحنفية تعم سنة الخلفاء الراشدين لكنه حجة عندهم فإن سنة الخلفاء حجة عندهم أيضاً والنزاع في أن لفظ السنة في إطلاق الصحابة لأي سنة فعندنا المتبادر منها طريقة مسلوكة في الدين سواء كانت طريقة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أو طريقة الخلفاء الراشدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم لنا أن السنة لغة الطريقة، ثم عرفا الطريقة الحسنة، ثم طريان النقل لم يثبت بل خلاف الأصل فيبقى إطلاقهم على العرف العام، ويؤيده قول أمير المؤمنين علي رضي الله عنه (وقيل: ليس بحجة لأنه ليس واحداً من الثلاثة لأنه إنما يدل على أن فعلهم كذا لا أنه تعالى أو الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ولا إجماع أيضاً (وإلا كان المخالفة) إياه (خرقاً للإجماع) فيكون الخلاف يسمع) نحو قول ابن عمر: كنا نتخير في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فخيرنا أبا بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان، رواه البخاري وقول أبي هريرة كنا نقول ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم حي : أفضل الناس أبو بكر ، ثم عمر ثم عثمان (فرفع إلى الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (بلا توقف) فيه (هذا).
Issue: For the wording of the Companion in narration there are seven degrees. First: 'He said to us', 'He informed me', 'He narrated to us', and similar – these are proof without dispute because these words are apparent in direct hearing unless there is a contrary indicator. Second: 'He said upon him' and peace – it is taken as hearing because the apparent state of the Companion is that he only attributes the statement to [the Prophet] through hearing. Al-Baqillani said it is not taken as hearing but also admits the possibility of irsal. Third: 'He commanded and forbade' – most say it is proof because the apparent is that the commander is the Prophet orally. There is dispute regarding the implication of command and prohibition. Fourth: Statement of a ruling in passive form like 'we were commanded' and 'it was forbidden to us'. Fifth: 'From the Sunnah' – it is proof for most because apparent in his Sunnah; for Hanafis it includes the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Sixth: 'From the Prophet' using 'an' – majority consider it as hearing; but the correct view is that it is only attribution, not direct hearing unless based on the question of 'adalah. Seventh: 'We used to do' and similar – apparent in reporting consensus; if it includes 'in his time' or 'he hears', then it is raised to the Prophet without pause.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the seven degrees of wording used by the Companions when transmitting hadith. It is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. The author explains each degree and its probative value (hujjiyyah) with scholarly disagreements.

First degree: Words like 'qala lana' (he said to us) are universally accepted as indicating direct hearing because they are explicit in that sense, unless there is an indicator otherwise. The author cites examples from Hasan al-Basri and the story of the Dajjal to illustrate that even if direct meeting is questionable, the wording still implies hearing.

Second degree: 'Qala 'alayhi' (he said upon him) – most hold it implies hearing because the Companion would not attribute a statement to the Prophet without hearing it. However, al-Baqillani argues it could be mursal (from a subordinate), so its acceptance depends on the principle that all Companions are 'udul (just). The author refutes the claim that Companions never narrated from tabi'un by mentioning Ka'b al-Ahbar and the treatise of al-Suyuti.

Third degree: 'Amara wa naha' (he commanded and forbade) – the majority consider it proof because it is apparent that the command comes from the Prophet directly. Some, like Imam Abu Hanifa, hesitated because these forms might imply recommendation or prohibition without legal force. The author argues that the probability of error is remote and does not negate the obvious meaning.

Fourth degree: Using passive verbs like 'umirna' (we were commanded) – this is weaker than the previous degrees because it could be interpreted as command from a ruler, the Book, or qiyas. For example, the hadith of Umm 'Atiyya 'we were commanded' was understood by the Hanafis as indicating something less than obligation.

Fifth degree: 'Min al-sunnah' (from the Sunnah) – for most it is proof because it clearly refers to the Prophet's way. The Hanafis extend it to include the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs based on the linguistic and customary usage of 'sunnah' as a good path, supported by the hadith of 'Ali about lashing punishments.

Sixth degree: 'An al-nabi' (from the Prophet) using the particle 'an – Ibn al-Salah and a group take it as direct hearing based on common usage. However, the author sides with the majority of usul scholars who say 'an only indicates attribution (mansub ilayh), not necessarily oral hearing. Still, it is accepted as evidence due to the presumption of 'adalah.

Seventh degree: 'Kunna naf'alu' (we used to do) – this is apparent in reporting a consensus of the Companions. Some argue it is not proof because it only describes their action, not a command. But if the phrase is accompanied by a temporal indicator like 'fi 'ahdihi' (in his time) or 'wa huwa yasma'u' (and he hears), then it is raised to the Prophet and becomes definitive evidence without pause. The author illustrates with the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and Abu Huraira regarding the preference for Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthman.

Key Terms
كنا نفعل (kunnā naf'alu): Seventh degree: Companion reports a practice of the group

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the seventh degree 'kunnā naf'alu' become a direct proof from the Prophet without hesitation?
A) When it is narrated by a major Companion.
B) When it includes 'fi 'ahdihi' or 'wa huwa yasma'u'.
C) When it is found in Sahih al-Bukhari.

Key Takeaway
The seventh degree is a valuable source of law, especially when it connects to the Prophet's era.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate

Fifth Degree of Sunnah: Hanafi Definition and Scope
Defines Sunnah and argues for inclusion of Rightly Guided Caliphs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Sunnah according to the Hanafi school and explain why the actions of the Rightly Guided Caliphs are included as proof.

Core Idea
Hanafi Sunnah is a path in religion, encompassing the Prophet's and the Caliphs' ways, both binding as proof.

In this passage, the author addresses a key point of dispute: what does 'Sunnah' mean? For the majority, it is the Prophet's way alone, but the Hanafis argue that the term, as used by the Companions, includes the path of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and textual evidence for the Hanafi position, showing how Sunnah functions as a source of law (dalil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of Sunnah in the passage
Locate how the Hanafi school defines Sunnah—not just the Prophet's actions but any path followed in religion.
The definition is rooted in linguistic and customary usage.
2
Examine the Hanafi claim regarding the Caliphs
The passage states that the fifth degree of Sunnah includes the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and is proof (hujja) for Hanafis.
This is the key point of difference with other schools.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
The author argues that 'Sunnah' originally means 'a path' (tariqa) and in custom means 'a good path'. No transfer (naql) to a specific meaning has been established, so the general usage remains.
This relies on the usul principle that the original meaning is presumed unless transfer is proven.
4
Study the hadith proof from Ali
The hadith from Ali (r) refers to floggings by the Prophet (40), Abu Bakr (40), and Umar (80) as 'all Sunnah', showing that the term includes Caliphs' actions.
This is a direct textual evidence (dalil naqli) supporting the Hanafi position.
5
Contrast with the opposing view
The majority restricts Sunnah to the Prophet alone. The Hanafi response is that no transfer of meaning has occurred, so the general custom remains authoritative.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) affects rulings like Umar's triple talaq being considered Sunnah.

الأصل الثاني/ السنة اتباع الجنائز (والخلاف فيه أقوى من السابقة للزيادة بانضمام احتمال كون الحاكم بعض الأئمة أو الكتاب أو القياس الظاهر إسقاط الكتاب لأنه لا ينافي الحجية، قيل: لا يحتمل الخلاف في أمير المؤمنين الصديق فإنه لم يكن إمام فوقه حتى يأمره وفيه أن احتمال القياس باق. الدرجة الخامسة من السنة) وهو (حجة عند الأكثر للظهور في سنته عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) وعند الحنفية تعم سنة الخلفاء الراشدين لكنه حجة عندهم فإن سنة الخلفاء حجة عندهم أيضاً والنزاع في أن لفظ السنة في إطلاق الصحابة لأي سنة فعندنا المتبادر منها طريقة مسلوكة في الدين سواء كانت طريقة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أو طريقة الخلفاء الراشدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم لنا أن السنة لغة الطريقة، ثم عرفا الطريقة الحسنة، ثم طريان النقل لم يثبت بل خلاف الأصل فيبقى إطلاقهم على العرف العام، ويؤيده قول أمير المؤمنين علي رضي الله عنه وعن آله الكرام جلد النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أربعين وأبو بكر أربعين وعمر ثمانين وكل سنة رواه مسلم
The second source: Sunnah. [Discussion of following funerals? The disagreement in it is stronger than the previous due to the addition of the possibility that the ruler is one of the imams or the Book or apparent analogy that drops the Book because it does not negate authority. It was said: There is no possibility of disagreement in the case of the Commander of the Faithful al-Siddiq because there was no imam above him to command him, but in it the possibility of analogy remains.] The fifth degree of Sunnah: It is proof for the majority due to its apparent meaning in his Sunnah, peace be upon him and his family and companions. According to the Hanafis, it includes the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, but it is proof for them, for the Sunnah of the caliphs is also proof for them. The dispute is about the word 'Sunnah' in the usage of the Companions: which Sunnah? According to us, what is immediately understood is a path followed in the religion, whether it is the path of the Messenger of Allah or the path of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Our proof: Sunnah linguistically means a path, then customarily a good path, then transference [to a specific meaning] has not been established, rather it is contrary to the original principle, so their usage remains on the general custom. Supporting this is the statement of the Commander of the Faithful Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, flogged forty, Abu Bakr forty, and Umar eighty, and all are Sunnah.' Narrated by Muslim.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and definition: it establishes that the Sunnah is a source of proof and clarifies its scope according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by noting that the second source (Sunnah) involves stronger disagreement than the first (the Quran) because in addition to the possibility of the ruler being one of the imams (as in the question of following funeral processions), there is also the possibility of analogy overriding the apparent meaning of the Book. A counterargument is raised that no such disagreement applies to Abu Bakr since there was no imam above him to command him, but the author notes that the possibility of analogy still remains. The fifth degree of Sunnah is defined: it is proof for the majority due to its apparent meaning. Hanafis specifically include the Sunnah of the Rightly Guided Caliphs within this degree, considering it also proof. The dispute centers on what 'Sunnah' meant in the Companions' usage. The Hanafi position is that the immediate understanding is a general religious path, encompassing both the Prophet's way and the Caliphs' way. The linguistic argument is that 'Sunnah' originally means a path, and in customary usage it means a good path; there is no evidence of a narrow transfer, so the general custom remains. The proof is a hadith from Ali where he calls the different floggings of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and Umar all 'Sunnah', showing that the term includes the Caliphs' actions.

Key Terms
السنة (al-Sunnah): A path followed in religion, including the way of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs.
الخلفاء الراشدين (al-Khulafa' al-Rashidun): The four Rightly Guided Caliphs: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of Sunnah is broader than the Prophet alone—it includes the Rightly Guided Caliphs as a source of law, supported by language and hadith.

The Meaning of 'An' in Hadith Transmission
Textual analysis of the word 'an' in hadith transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi view on whether 'an' implies direct hearing and explain why it is still accepted as proof.

Core Idea
The word 'an' only attributes a narration to the Prophet; it does not prove direct hearing.

In hadith studies, the word 'an' (from) is commonly used by Companions when narrating from the Prophet. Does this guarantee that the Companion heard the hadith directly? Ibn al-Salah and some scholars say yes, but the majority of usul scholars, including the Hanafis, hold that 'an' merely indicates attribution, not direct hearing. However, such narrations are still accepted as proof due to the uprightness of the Companions (ta'dil). This lesson unpacks the linguistic and methodological reasoning behind this key usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under analysis
The term is 'an' (عن) as used by a Companion when narrating from the Prophet. Determine what it literally indicates.
The word is a preposition, not a verb of hearing.
2
State the two positions
Ibn al-Salah and a group hold that 'an' implies direct hearing (sama'), because that is the apparent state of a reliable Companion. The majority of usul scholars hold that it may involve an intermediary (irsal), i.e., the Companion could be narrating from another Companion.
The Hanafi school follows the majority view.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
The word 'an' only indicates that the narration is from the Prophet and attributed to him. It does not specify the mode of transmission. Direct hearing is an additional claim that requires separate proof.
The author declares the majority view as correct.
4
Understand the Hanafi acceptance
Despite the linguistic ambiguity, the hadith is still considered proof (hujja) because of the principle of ta'dil: Companions are upright and reliable, so even if the chain is not explicitly direct, it is taken as trustworthy.
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude with the operative rule
In Hanafi usul, a hadith transmitted by a Companion using the word 'an' is accepted as proof, even though it does not necessarily prove direct hearing. The presumption is that the Companion would not narrate without a proper chain.
This affects how Hanafis weigh hadith evidence in deriving rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدرجة (السادسة : عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله أصحابه وسلم ، فابن الصلاح وجماعة حملوه على السماع إذ هو الظاهر من حال الصحابي والأكثر) من أهل الأصول (على احتمال الإرسال) يعني أن السماع بواسطة محتمل وليس بظن سماع بلا واسطة وهو الحق، لأن كلمة عن على أنه مروي عنه ومنسوب إليه وأما أنه مسموع منه فأمر زائد لا يحتمله اللفظ، فإثباته من غير دليل، لكن يكون حجة بناء على مسألة التعديل .
The sixth degree: From the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family and companions. Ibn al-Salah and a group interpreted it as hearing (sama') because that is the apparent state of the Companion. But the majority of the scholars of usul hold it as possibly being with an intermediary (irsal), meaning that hearing through an intermediary is possible and it is not a presumption of hearing without an intermediary. This is the correct view, because the word 'عن' indicates that it is narrated from him and attributed to him. As to it being heard from him, that is an additional matter not implied by the wording, so establishing it without proof [is not allowed]. However, it is a proof based on the issue of ta'dil (companions' reliability).
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'عن' in hadith narration. The author discusses the sixth degree of Sunnah, which is the hadith transmitted by a Companion using the word 'عن' (from the Prophet). Ibn al-Salah and some scholars hold that such a phrase implies direct hearing from the Prophet, because a Companion is presumed to be reliable and would not narrate without hearing. However, the majority of usul scholars hold that 'عن' only indicates that the hadith is traced back to the Prophet, but it does not necessarily imply direct hearing; an intermediary is possible. The author declares the majority view as correct because the word 'عن' merely indicates attribution, not direct hearing. However, such a hadith is still considered a proof because of the issue of ta'dil (the uprightness of the Companions), meaning that the Companion is trustworthy and would not narrate without a proper chain, even if it is not direct. This is a key point in Hanafi usul: hadith with 'عن' are accepted as proof even if not directly heard, due to the reliability of the narrators.

Key Terms
عن ('an): A preposition used in hadith transmission that indicates the narration is from the Prophet but not necessarily directly heard.
السماع (al-sama'): Direct hearing of the hadith from the Prophet without an intermediary.
التعديل (al-ta'dil): The principle that Companions are reliable and upright; their narrations are accepted even without explicit confirmation of direct hearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It definitively indicates direct hearing from the Prophet.
B) It indicates the narration is from the Prophet but may involve an intermediary.
C) It indicates the narration is weak and unreliable.
D) It indicates the Companion is unknown.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'an' in hadith indicates attribution, not direct hearing, but such narrations are accepted as proof due to the reliability of the Companions.

The Legal Weight of 'Kana Naf'alu' – Speculative Consensus and Prophetic Approval
Ruling on the legal value of a Companion saying 'we used to do'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between speculative (zanni) and definitive (qat'i) consensus to evaluate a Companion's statement 'we used to do' (kana naf'alu) and identify when an added phrase indicating prophetic presence elevates it to a stronger proof.

Core Idea
A Companion's 'we used to do' is speculative consensus; opposition is not error – but with added prophetic context, it becomes direct proof.

In this lesson, we examine the seventh degree of proof in Hanafi usul: a Companion's statement 'we used to do' (kana naf'alu). The Hanafi school treats such statements as apparent evidence for consensus (ijma'), but only speculative (zanni), not definitive. We will see why opposing this type of consensus does not nullify the opponent, and how a phrase like 'in the era of the Prophet' or 'while he was hearing' transforms it into a statement traceable to the Prophet himself, thus becoming a stronger proof for a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and counter-claim regarding 'kana naf'alu'
Locate the two positions in the passage: the Hanafi view that it is apparent in transmitting consensus, and the opposing view that it is not proof at all because it only indicates the Companions' action.
The Hanafi view is that it is speculative consensus; opponents say it is not proof.
2
Distinguish between definitive and speculative consensus
Understand the key distinction: if the consensus were definitive (qat'i), opposing it would be a breach (kharq) and error. But since this consensus is speculative (zanni), opposition is not invalidating.
This distinction is central to the Hanafi response to the objection.
3
Determine the effect of adding a phrase indicating prophetic presence
Analyze the examples: when a Companion says 'we used to do' with an addition like 'in the era of the Messenger of Allah' or 'while he was hearing,' the statement is elevated to the Prophet without hesitation, implying his approval.
Examples from Ibn Umar and Abu Huraira illustrate this stronger form.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle to the given examples
For each example, classify it as either general (speculative consensus) or specific (prophetic proof). See how the ruling changes based on the presence of the additional context.
The general form is a proof but not binding; the specific form is a direct evidence for a sunnah.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Note the practical difference: Hanafis accept the general 'we used to do' as evidence in ijtihad, while opponents may reject it entirely. The specific form with prophetic context is accepted by both.
Example: the practice of choosing Abu Bakr as caliph is based on such a statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدرجة (السابعة) قول الصحابي (كنا نفعل ونحوه) وهو (ظاهر في نقل الاجماع فالمعنى كنا جماعة الصحابة نفعل جميعاً (وقيل: ليس بحجة لأنه ليس واحداً من الثلاثة لأنه إنما يدل على أن فعلهم كذا لا أنه تعالى أو الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ولا إجماع أيضاً (وإلا كان المخالفة) إياه (خرقاً للإجماع) فيكون الخلاف خطأ، وهو باطل بالإجماع، فإنه لا يخطىء مخالفة (والجواب أن ذلك أي بطلان خرق الإجماع (في) الإجماع (القطعي) وهذا الإجماع ظني، فلا يكون المخالف مبطلاً ( وأما ) قوله : كنا نفعل بزيادة نحو في عهده أو هو يسمع) نحو قول ابن عمر: كنا نتخير في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فخيرنا أبا بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان، رواه البخاري وقول أبي هريرة كنا نقول ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم حي : أفضل الناس أبو بكر ، ثم عمر ثم عثمان (فرفع إلى الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (بلا توقف) فيه (هذا).
The seventh degree: The saying of the Companion 'we used to do' and similar. It is apparent in transmitting consensus (ijma'), so the meaning is 'we, the group of Companions, all used to do.' It was said: It is not proof because it is not one of the three [sources] because it only indicates their action, not that it is from Allah or the Messenger, nor is it consensus. Otherwise, opposing it would be a breach of consensus, and that is false by consensus, for opposing consensus is error. The answer: That nullification of breaching consensus applies to definitive consensus, and this consensus is speculative, so the opponent is not nullified. As for his statement 'we used to do' with an addition like 'in his era' or 'he was hearing,' like Ibn Umar's saying: 'We used to choose in the era of the Messenger of Allah, so we chose Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman' (narrated by Bukhari), and Abu Huraira's saying: 'We used to say while the Messenger of Allah was alive: The best of people is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman' — this is elevated to the Messenger of Allah without hesitation.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal weight of a Companion's statement 'we used to do' (kana naf'alu). The author explains that such a statement is apparently a transmission of consensus, meaning that all Companions did that action. Some argued that it is not proof because it does not come from Allah or the Prophet and is not binding consensus. They said that opposing it would be breaching consensus, but the answer is that this nullification only applies to definitive consensus, while this kind of consensus is speculative, so the opponent is not considered to have committed an error. The author then distinguishes a stronger form: if the Companion adds 'in his era' or 'while he was hearing,' such as the examples from Ibn Umar and Abu Huraira, then the statement is considered traceable to the Prophet without hesitation—implying his approval—and thus it becomes a proof. So the seventh degree includes two types: a general 'we used to do' which is speculative consensus, and a specific one with implied prophetic approval which is a stronger proof.

Key Terms
كنا نفعل (kana naf'alu): A Companion's statement 'we used to do' – considered apparent in transmitting consensus of the Companions.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids. Here, consensus of the Companions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is definitive consensus and cannot be opposed
B) It is not proof at all
C) It is speculative consensus, so opposing does not nullify the opponent
D) It is a direct report from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
A Companion's 'we used to do' is apparent in transmitting consensus, but only speculative; with an added prophetic context, it becomes directly traceable to the Prophet.

The Hanafi Rule on When a Companion’s Practice Contradicts a Hadith
Sets the condition for when a Companion's practice harms a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which a Companion's contrary practice harms a hadith and apply the Hanafi distinction between obscure and well-known rulings.

Core Idea
A Companion’s practice only harms a hadith if the ruling was well-known; obscure rulings are exempt.

In usul al-fiqh, the practice of a Companion carries special weight because of his direct witness of the Prophet’s context. But what happens when a Companion’s practice contradicts a hadith he or another Companion narrated? Does that automatically weaken the hadith? The Hanafis draw a crucial distinction: it depends on whether the ruling was likely known to the acting Companion. This lesson unpacks that rule through two classic examples: loud laughter in prayer (qahqaha) and exile for fornication (taghrib).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Companion from non-Companion narrators
The author first refutes a false analogy claiming that a Companion's practice contradicting his narration is like any narrator's contradiction. The key difference: a Companion observed the Prophet's context, so his practice has evidentiary weight a later narrator lacks.
This is a 'qiyas ma'a al-fariq' — an analogy with a difference.
2
Identify the two categories of rulings
Hanafis split cases into two: rulings that are obscure (khafi) and likely unknown to the Companion, and rulings that are well-known (ghayr khafi) and could not have been missed.
The distinction hinges on probability of knowledge.
3
Apply to the qahqaha (loud laughter) hadith
The hadith says loud laughter breaks wudu and prayer. Some Companions reportedly did not act on it. The Hanafis argue this does not harm the hadith because the Companions' deep humility in prayer made qahqaha virtually impossible, so they never needed to learn the ruling — it was obscure to them.
The rarity of the event makes ignorance plausible.
4
Apply to the taghrib (exile) hadith
The hadith prescribes exile for virgin fornicators. Yet 'Umar and 'Ali did not implement it. Here the ruling is well-known and frequently applicable, so their contrary practice harms the hadith's authenticity, leading the Hanafis to treat exile as ta'zir not hadd.
The commonness of the ruling negates the excuse of ignorance.
5
Synthesize the principle
The rule: A Companion's contrary practice only undermines a hadith if the ruling was so well-known that the Companion could not have been unaware. Otherwise, the hadith remains valid.
This preserves the integrity of many ahadith while allowing critical evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل في حواشي مرزاجان خلاف عمل الصحابي خلاف روايته (مثل عمل غيره ممن روى الحديث) العادل فيجب أن يعتبر ويتبع، وهو باطل (فإنه لا يعتبر اتفاقاً، أقول) هو (قياس مع الفارق، لأن الرواة ليس لهم إلا الرواية ولا علم لهم بالقرائن والأسماع (بخلاف الصحابي فله المشاهدة والسماع وبهما العبرة كما لا يخفى فلا يتناول الحجية لراوي الحديث مطلقاً (ومن ثمة اعتبر الصحابي (في حمل المجمل اتفاقاً بينكم أيها الخصوم وإن كنا لا نوافقكم فيه، ولم يعتبر غيره من الرواة فتدبر، وإن عمل بخلاف خبره غيره فإن كان صحابياً فالحنفية قالوا : (إن كان) الخبر (مما يحتمل الخفاء على العامل كحديث القهقهة فإنه روى معبد الخزاعي صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه قال : من كان منكم قهقه فليعد الوضوء والصلاة رواه الإمام أبو حنيفة فعن أبي موسى الأشعري روى (تركه) فالتارك غير الراوي. قال في التيسير : قد يمنع صحة الرواية عنه، بل روى الطبراني عنه مرفوعاً بإسناد صحيح خلافه (لا يضر عمل هذا الصحابي العمل بالحديث (لأنه) أي ما يحتمل الخفاء (من الحوادث النادرة) فيحتمل أن يكون تركه لعدم العلم بالحديث فلا يورث ضعفاً في الحديث. وهذا ظاهر جداً في حديث القهقهة، فإن الصحابة من كرام أولياء الله تعالى. وخشوعهم في الصلاة والمشاهدة فيها أتم وفوق ما لغيرهم، فلا يشغلهم شأن عن المشاهدة والخشوع فلا مجال لاحتمال القهقهة الصلاة، ألا ترى أنه لم يحك عنهم قهقهة خارج الصلاة إلا ما يكون أندر عن البعض الذي في أنه لم يكن له صحبة طويلة، فكيف يقع في الصلاة؟ فهم لا يحتاجون إلى معرفة حكم القهقهة فيحتمل الخفاء فافهم (وإلا) أي وإن كان لا يحتمل الخفاء على العامل (فيقدح) في الحديث المروي كحديث التغريب وهو ما روى مسلم وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجه والشافعي وعبد الرزاق وابن أبي شيبة والطحاوي عن عبادة بن الصامت: «خذوا عني قد جعل الله لهن سبيلاً الثيب بالثيب جلد مائة ورجم بالحجارة والبكر بالبكر جلد مائة ثم نفي سنة» (حلف) أمير المؤمنين (عمر) أن لا ينفي أبداً بعد لحاق من غربه مرتداً بالروم) روى عبد الرزاق عن ابن المسيب قال : غرب عمر رضي الله عنه ربيعة بن أمية بن خلف إلى خيبر، فلحق بهرقل فتنصر، فقال : لا أغرب بعده مسلماً (وقال) أمير المؤمنين (علي) كرم الله وجهه ووجوه آله
It was said in the glosses of Marzajan: The contradiction of the practice of a Companion to his narration (is like the practice of other than him who narrated a hadith) of the upright, so it must be considered and followed. But it is false, for it is not considered by agreement. I say: It is an analogical argument with a difference, for the narrators have only narration and no knowledge of contexts and hearings, contrary to the Companion, for he has observation and hearing, and they are the criteria, as is not hidden. So authority does not apply to the narrator of a hadith absolutely. Hence the Companion is considered in carrying the mujmal by agreement between you opponents, though we do not agree with you in that. And other narrators are not considered, so ponder. And if another [Companion] acts contrary to a hadith of a [different] Companion, then if it is a Companion, the Hanafis said: If the report is such that it is possible it was obscure to the actor, like the hadith of loud laughter (qahqaha), for it was narrated by Ma'bad al-Khuza'i, peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions, who said: Whoever among you laughs loudly, let him repeat wudu and prayer. It was narrated by Imam Abu Hanifa, and from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari it is narrated that he abandoned it, so the abandoner is not the narrator. It is said in al-Taysir: The authenticity of the narration from him may be denied; rather, al-Tabarani narrated from him with a sound chain contrary to it. This practice of the Companion does not harm acting upon the hadith, because what is possible to be obscure is from rare occurrences, so it is possible that his abandonment was due to lack of knowledge of the hadith, so it does not cause weakness in the hadith. This is very apparent in the hadith of loud laughter, for the Companions are among the noble saints of Allah. Their humility in prayer and observation therein is complete and beyond that of others, so no matter distracts them from observation and humility, so there is no room for the possibility of loud laughter in prayer. Do you not see that it is not reported from them laughter outside prayer except what is extremely rare from some who did not have a long companionship? So how could it occur in prayer? They do not need to know the ruling of loud laughter, so it is possible that it was obscure. So understand. Otherwise, i.e., if it is not possible that it was obscure to the actor, then it harms the narrated hadith, like the hadith of exile (taghrib). This is what Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Shafi'i, Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shayba, and al-Tahawi narrated from Ubada ibn al-Samit: 'Take from me; Allah has made a way for them: the previously-married with the previously-married, a hundred lashes and stoning with stones; the virgin with the virgin, a hundred lashes and exile for a year.' The Commander of the Faithful, 'Umar, swore that he would never exile after the one he exiled joined the Romans and apostatized. Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Ibn al-Musayyab: 'Umar exiled Rabi'a ibn Umayya ibn Khalaf to Khaybar, he joined Heraclius and became a Christian, then 'Umar said: I will not exile a Muslim after him. And the Commander of the Faithful, 'Ali, may Allah honor his face and the faces of his family, said...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of when a Companion's practice contradicts a hadith that he or another Companion narrated. The author first refutes the idea that this contradiction is like that of a non-Companion narrator; the Companion's practice is given special weight because he witnessed the context and may have heard evidence not available to later narrators. The discussion then splits into two cases: if the hadith concerns a rare occurrence that could have been obscure to the acting Companion (like the hadith on loud laughter in prayer), then the contradiction does not harm the hadith because the Companion may have been unaware of it. The example of the qahqaha hadith illustrates this: the Companions' deep humility in prayer made loud laughter extremely unlikely, so they had no need to learn its ruling. In contrast, if the ruling is well-known and not obscure, as in the hadith on exile for fornication (taghrib), then a Companion's contrary practice (e.g., 'Umar and 'Ali not implementing exile) harms the hadith's authenticity. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'Ali's statement, indicating a continuation.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter in prayer, which according to some hadith invalidates wudu and prayer.
التغريب (at-taghrib): Exile as a punishment for fornicators who are unmarried (virgins).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the practice of some Companions contrary to the hadith of qahqaha not harm the hadith according to the Hanafis?
A) Because the hadith is weak anyway
B) Because the ruling was obscure and unlikely to affect the Companions due to their deep humility in prayer
C) Because the hadith contradicts the Quran
D) Because the Companions who acted contrary were not reliable

Key Takeaway
A Companion's contrary practice harms a hadith only if the ruling was well-known and the Companion's ignorance is implausible; otherwise, the hadith remains valid.

The Hanafi Rule on Companion's Action Contrary to His Narration
Usul principle on companion action vs. narration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the Hanafi principle that a companion's contrary action only impugns a narration if the ruling was not obscure, and DISTINGUISH the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
A companion's action only weakens his narration if the ruling was well-known, not if it was obscure.

In this passage, the author examines the legal authority of a companion's narration when his own practice contradicts it. The Hanafis argue that such contradiction does not automatically invalidate the hadith; rather, the key is whether the companion could have been unaware of the ruling. This principle has significant implications for accepting ahad hadiths that were not universally practiced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core usul question
What is the legal effect of a companion (sahabi) acting contrary to a narration he himself transmitted? The Hanafi school addresses this by considering the nature of the ruling.
2
State the Hanafi rule
If the narration concerns a rare matter (e.g., laughter breaking wudu), the companion's inaction does not harm the hadith. If the matter is well-known (e.g., banishment for fornication), the contrary action impugns the hadith.
Key criterion: 'what could be hidden from the companion' (ما يحتمل الخفاء)
3
Analyze the rationale
Companions have direct observation (mushahada) and hearing (sama') of the Prophet's practice, giving them contextual knowledge that other narrators lack. Their action may be based on evidence (e.g., abrogation, policy) rather than rejection of the hadith.
Other transmitters only have the text; companions have additional information.
4
Examine the examples
Apply the rule to the hadith of laughter (rare, so companion's inaction does not weaken) and the hadith of banishment (well-known, so Umar and Ali's contrary actions indicate a problem).
The text suggests Umar's banishment of Rabi'a may have been for policy, not as a fixed hadd.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i generally holds that a companion's practical rejection weakens the hadith, whereas Hanafis require the companion to have knowledge of the ruling for his action to impugn.
This leads to different outcomes in fiqh, e.g., acceptance of the laughter hadith in Hanafi school.

الصلاة إلا ما يكون أندر عن البعض الذي لم يكن له صحبة طويلة، فكيف يقع في الصلاة؟ فهم لا يحتاجون إلى معرفة حكم القهقهة فيحتمل الخفاء فافهم (وإلا) أي وإن كان لا يحتمل الخفاء على العامل (فيقدح) في الحديث المروي كحديث التغريب وهو ما روى مسلم وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجه والشافعي وعبد الرزاق وابن أبي شيبة والطحاوي عن عبادة بن الصامت: «خذوا عني قد جعل الله لهن سبيلاً الثيب بالثيب جلد مائة ورجم بالحجارة والبكر بالبكر جلد مائة ثم نفي سنة» (حلف) أمير المؤمنين (عمر) أن لا ينفي أبداً بعد لحاق من غربه مرتداً بالروم) روى عبد الرزاق عن ابن المسيب قال : غرب عمر رضي الله عنه ربيعة بن أمية بن خلف إلى الله بن مسعود في البكر يزني : يجلدان مائة وينفيان سنة. قال: قال علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله تعالى عنه : حسبهما من الفتنة أن ينفيا، وأما لفظ الكتاب فقول إبراهيم النخعي كما رواه ذلك الإمام فهذان الإمامان الهاديان المهديان عملا خلاف رواية عبادة وابن مسعود (ومثله لا يخفى عن مثلهما بل هما أولى بالعلم منهما بهذا الأمر، فإن الخليفة أحق بمعرفة أمر الحد، لأنه المأمور بالإقامة، وأما تغريب أمير المؤمنين عمر The refutation of the evidence is rejected because what is meant is preponderance by sensory perception usually, so understand. That means that most of the time his action is based on hearing or the circumstantial evidence of witnessing his uprightness. Thus, what is understood from him is the intent of Allah and His Messenger, and we do not claim certainty of it, for acting on probability is obligatory, and this possibility does not contradict that. Rather we say: this possibility does not arise from evidence, especially regarding the Caliphs and the leading Companions, so it is not considered. If a Companion abandons an unambiguous text that admits no interpretation, then his knowledge of an abrogator is established, because opposing an unambiguous text could be a major sin, and the Companion is too exalted to commit it, and it does not admit interpretation to be considered interpretable. Thus abrogation is determined exclusively. Then either his knowledge of abrogation is erroneous or correct; the first is invalid as he indicated. And the possibility of his deeming what is not abrogating as abrogating is more remote than correctness, for an abrogator of an unambiguous text can only be like it, so it does not admit error. Thus the second is determined, so following it is obligatory, contrary to al-Shafi'i may Allah have mercy on him, due to what settled in his opinion as mentioned. It was said in the marginalia of Mirzajan: The action of a Companion contrary to his narration is like the action of any upright transmitter, so it must be considered and followed, and that is false. For it is not considered by consensus. I say: it is an analogy with a difference, because transmitters have only transmission, no knowledge of circumstantial evidence and hearings, unlike the Companion, who has observation and hearing, and these are the criteria, as is clear. Thus the authority does not apply to any transmitter of hadith absolutely. Hence the Companion is considered in interpreting the general text by consensus among you opponents, though we do not agree with you therein, and other transmitters are not considered, so ponder. If another acts contrary to his report, if it is a Companion, the Hanafis said: If the report is such that it could be hidden from the actor, like the hadith of laughter, for al-Khuzai reported, 'Whoever among you laughed, let him repeat the ablution and prayer.' Imam Abu Hanifa reported it from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that he left it, so the leaver is not the narrator. Al-Taysir said: The authenticity of the report from him may be denied; rather al-Tabarani reported from him as marfu' with a sound chain contrary to it. The action of this Companion does not harm acting on the hadith, because what could be hidden is from rare incidents; it is possible that his leaving was due to ignorance of the hadith, so it does not cause weakness in the hadith. This is very clear in the hadith of laughter, for the Companions are the noble saints of Allah, and their humility in prayer and observation therein is complete and beyond that of others, so no concern distracts them from observation and humility. Thus there is no room for the possibility of laughter in prayer. Do you not see that laughter outside prayer is not reported from them except as something extremely rare from someone who did not have long companionship? So how could it occur in prayer? They do not need to know the ruling of laughter, so it could be hidden, so understand. Otherwise, meaning if it could not be hidden from the actor, then it impugns the reported hadith, like the hadith of banishment, which is what Muslim, Abu Dawood, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Shafi'i, Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and al-Tahawi reported from Ubadah ibn al-Samit: 'Take from me: Allah has made a way for them: the married fornicator and the married fornicatress: one hundred lashes and stoning with stones; the unmarried and the unmarried: one hundred lashes and then exile for a year.' The Commander of the Faithful Umar swore that he would never exile after the one he exiled joined the Romans as an apostate. Abd al-Razzaq reported from Ibn al-Musayyib: Umar exiled Rabi'a ibn Umayya ibn Khalaf to Khaybar, and he joined Heraclius and converted to Christianity. Then he said: I will not exile a Muslim after him. And the Commander of the Faithful Ali, may Allah honor his face and the faces of his noble family, said: Sufficient as a trial is exile. Imam Muhammad reported through Abu Hanifa from Hammad from Ibrahim that Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said regarding the unmarried fornicator: they are flogged one hundred lashes and exiled for one year. He said: Ali ibn Abi Talib said: Sufficient for them as trial is that they are exiled. As for the wording of the book, it is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i as reported by that Imam. So these two guided and rightly guiding Imams acted contrary to the report of Ubadah and Ibn Mas'ud. And the like is not hidden from the like of them; rather they are more entitled to knowledge of this matter, for the caliph is more deserving of knowing the matter of the hadd, because he is commanded to execute it. As for the banishment of Umar, perhaps it was for policy, not as a hadd. So understand and reflect on it, for it is not free from unease.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi usul principle regarding when a companion's action contradicts a narration he reports. The author argues that if the companion abandons an unambiguous text (nass mufassar), it must be due to knowledge of abrogation, because the companion is too upright to contradict it without proof. This contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i, who would consider such contradiction as weakening the narration. The Hanafi position is that if the narration concerns a rare matter that could be hidden from the companion (e.g., the hadith of laughter breaking wudu), his contrary action does not harm the hadith's authority. However, if the matter is well-known and cannot be hidden (e.g., the hadith on banishment for fornication), then the companion's contradictory action impugns the hadith. The text provides examples: the hadith of qahqaha (laughter) is considered rare because companions were deeply focused in prayer, likely unaware of it; the hadith on banishment (taghrib) is well-known, and the actions of Umar and Ali contradicting it are cited, but the author suggests Umar's banishment might have been for policy rather than as a fixed hadd punishment. The principle is that a companion's action can only weaken a narration if the ruling was not obscure.

Key Terms
عمل الصحابي (amal al-sahabi): The action of a companion that may contradict a narration he transmitted
نص مفسر (nass mufassar): An unambiguous text that admits no interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the narration is about well-known matters
B) When the narration concerns a rare matter that could be hidden from the companion
C) When the companion is a caliph
D) Never; it always weakens the hadith

Key Takeaway
A companion's action only casts doubt on his narration if the ruling was not obscure – otherwise, his inaction is due to ignorance, not rejection.

Non- Companion's Contrary Action Does Not Affect Hadith
Ruling on non-companion's contrary action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a non-companion's contrary practice does not weaken a hadith and distinguish this from the Maliki position on the practice of Madinah.

Core Idea
A non-companion's contrary action does not undermine a hadith; only companions' actions may indicate a hidden defect.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi usul principle: when a hadith is authenticated, the fact that later scholars or even the majority of Muslims act contrary to it does not affect its authority. The only exception, according to the Malikis, is the consensus of Madinah. Let's see how Hanafi scholars distinguish between the special status of companions and others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor
Determine if the person acting contrary to the hadith is a companion or a non-companion (ghayr sahabi). The Hanafi rule applies specifically to non-companions.
Companions have special knowledge; their contrary action may indicate a hidden defect in the hadith, but not for others.
2
Apply the ruling
If the actor is a non-companion, the hadith remains a binding proof (hujja). The contrary action is not a proof and does not weaken the hadith.
This is based on the principle that the report is a proof while the action of a non-companion is not a proof.
3
Recognize the Maliki exception
The Malikis consider the consistent practice (amal) of the people of Madinah as a form of consensus (ijma'), which they treat as a stronger proof than a solitary hadith.
The Hanafis do not grant this special status to the practice of any city; they require explicit consensus of all mujtahids.
4
Evaluate the distinction
Reflect on why Hanafis give weight to companions' actions but not to later scholars. Companions witnessed the revelation; their silence or contrary action can indicate abrogation or another proof.
This reinforces the importance of differentiating between sahih and ghayr sahabi in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان هذا العامل غير صحابي ولو كان أكثر الأمة فالعمل بالخبر لا غير، لأن الخبر حجة وعمله ليس حجة. وليس أيضاً ثبوت الخبر مما لا يخفى عليه إلا اجتماع أهل المدينة عند المالكية فإنه إجماع وحجة ومقدم على الخبر عندهم.
And if this actor is not a companion, even if he is the majority of the nation, then acting on the report is the only course, because the report is a proof and his action is not a proof. Also, the establishedness of the report is not such that it is hidden from him, except for the consensus of the people of Madinah according to the Malikis, for it is a consensus and a proof and given precedence over the report in their view.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This brief passage clarifies that when the person acting contrary to a hadith is not a companion, his action does not undermine the authority of the hadith. The hadith remains a binding proof (hujja), and his contrary practice is not a proof. The only exception mentioned is the practice of the people of Madinah according to the Maliki school, which they consider as consensus (ijma') and thus a stronger proof than a solitary hadith. This distinction reinforces the special status of companions in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
غير صحابي (ghayr sahabi): Anyone other than a companion, including later scholars or common Muslims

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, if a non-companion acts contrary to a hadith, what is the ruling?
A) The hadith becomes weak and is not acted upon
B) The hadith remains a proof and must be acted upon
C) The action of the majority overrides the hadith
D) The hadith is rejected unless corroborated

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, only companions have the potential to affect a hadith's authority through their contrary practice; non-companions' actions have no such effect.

Components of Hadith Transmission: Tahammul, Ada', and Baqa'
Defining the three components of hadith transmission and their subcategories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of hadith transmission, distinguish between 'azima and rukhsa within each, and explain the Hanafi preference for the 'ard method.

Core Idea
Hadith transmission has three pillars: receiving, delivering, preserving; each has a strict and a concessional form.

This lesson explores the foundational structure of hadith transmission as articulated in Hanafi usul. You will learn how the authenticity and authority of a hadith depend on the method of its reception (tahammul) and delivery (ada'), and the balance between strict adherence and concession in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Locate the three terms in the Arabic text: التحمل (receiving), الأداء (delivering), and البقاء (preserving). Note that each component has both a strict form ('azima) and a concessional form (rukhsa).
The passage begins with 'تتقوم الرواية فينا بـ ثلاثة' (transmission consists of three).
2
Analyze the 'azima forms of tahammul
The original strict form is the shaykh reading from memory to the student. The substitute strict form is the shaykh reading from a book or the student reading back ('ard) to the shaykh. Understand why Abu Hanifa preferred 'ard when a book is involved.
Abu Hanifa's preference is stated as: 'ورجحه أي العرض أبو حنيفة' (Abu Hanifa preferred 'ard).
3
Examine the dispute on preference
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view with that of most hadith scholars who favor the shaykh's direct reading. Analyze the proof offered by the opposing side and the Hanafi response that the Prophet's example is not analogous.
The passage says: 'خلافاً لأكثر المحدثين' (contrary to most hadith scholars).
4
Understand the rukhsa forms
Identify the rukhsa (concession) for tahammul as ijaza (permission to narrate). Also note the substitute forms of kitab (letter) and risala (message) which are treated like direct communication in legal terms.
The passage ends with: 'والرخصة في الأول الإجازة' (the rukhsa in the first is ijaza).
5
Learn the conventions for wording in 'ada'
In 'ard, one may use 'haddathana' or 'akhbarana', while in kitab/risala only 'akhbarani' is used. Understand the rationale: lack of direct face-to-face encounter restricts certain terms.
The passage states: 'في الكتاب والرسالة أخبرني لا حدثني' (in the letter and message: akhbarani, not haddathani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: تتقوم الرواية فينا بـ ثلاثة (التحمل والأداء، والبقاء، وكل منها رخصة وعزيمة فالعزيمة في الأول أي لتحمل أصل وخلف، والأصل (قراءة الشيخ) عليك (من حفظه) بل التحمل به قيل هو أعلى مما عداه (اتفاقاً) وهو ظاهر (أو) قراءة الشيخ عن (كتاب وقراءتك) أيها المتحمل (أو) قراءة (غيرك عليه فيقرر ) الشيخ (ولو ظناً بأن يكون هناك قرينة تفيد ظن التقرير، وإن لم يقرر هو باللسان (وهو العرض) في الاصطلاح (ورجحه) أي العرض (أبو حنيفة) إذا كان القراءة عن كتاب لإفادته التمكن من ضبط المتن (والسند) وكمال العناية به وذهب جمع ومنهم البخاري إلى المساواة بينهما (خلافاً لأكثر المحدثين) فإنهم قالوا : قراءة الشيخ أرجح واستدلالهم بقراءة الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم على الصحابة دون قراءتهم عليه في غير محل النزاع فإنه ثمة لا يمكن القراءة من الصحابة فإن ما يوحى إليه لا يمكن المعرفة به من غير إخباره بخلاف ما نحن فيه فالفرق واضح، والخلف الكتاب والرسالة، وإليه أشار بقوله (والكتاب كالخطاب، والرسالة كالقراءة شرعاً وعرفا) فإذا كتب الشيخ حديثاً وأرسل به أو أرسل رسولاً ليقرأه على المرسل إليه وأجاز الرواية عن نفسه كفى، كما إذا أخبر مشافهة (والتعليق) أي تعليق قبول الكتاب على البينة ليشهدوا عند المكتوب إليه أنه كتاب فلان الشيخ (تضييق) في باب السنة من أبي حنيفة لكمال عنايته بأمرها وعظم احتياطه بها ألا ترى إلى أمير المؤمنين علي كيف يحلف الراوي؟ (والصحيح كفاية ظن الخط) في الكتاب (والصدق) في الرسالة، فإذا ظن المكتوب إليه أنه خط فلان الشيخ أو ظن المرسل إليه صدق الرسول في رسالته كفى لأن الاتباع بالظن واجب بخلاف كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، فإن التلبيس في المعاملات أكثر مما في السنن، فلا يقبل كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي من غير بينة ويصح في العرض أن يقول المحتمل حين الرواية حدثنا، وأخبرنا، وأنبأنا، ونبأنا مقيداً بالقراءة ومطلقاً على الأصح ، قال الحاكم على ذلك عهدنا أئمتنا، ونقله عن الأئمة الأربعة المجتهدين (و) يقول (في الكتاب والرسالة أخبرني لا حدثني، لصحة إطلاق الخبر عند عدم المشافهة دون التحديث ولعل هذا اصطلاح ((والرخصة) في الأول (الإجازة)
Issue: The transmission consists for us of three [components]: receiving, delivering, and preserving. Each of them has an 'azima (strict fulfillment) and a rukhsa (concession). The 'azima in the first (receiving) is original and substitute. The original is the shaykh reading to you from his memory; indeed receiving through it is said to be the highest of what is other than it by consensus, and that is apparent. Or [it is] the shaykh reading from a book and your reading (O receiver) or the reading of someone else to him, and the shaykh approves, even if only by presumption, i.e., there is a circumstantial indication giving presumption of approval, even if he does not verbally approve. This is the 'ard (reading back) in terminology. Abu Hanifa preferred 'ard when the reading is from a book because it gives ability to verify the text and chain and complete care with it. A group including Bukhari equated the two, contrary to most hadith scholars, who said the shaykh's reading is more preponderant. Their proof with the Prophet's reading to the companions and not their reading to him is not relevant to the point of dispute, because there the companions could not read since what was revealed could only be known from his informing, unlike our case, so the difference is clear. The substitute is a letter and a message. To this he alluded by saying: 'The letter is like direct address, and the message is like reading, legally and customarily.' So if the shaykh writes a hadith and sends it, or sends a messenger to read it to the recipient, and permits narration from himself, it suffices, just as if he had informed directly. And requiring a condition (ta'liq), i.e., making acceptance of the letter conditional on witnesses to testify to the recipient that it is the letter of so-and-so the shaykh, is a narrow restriction in the chapter of sunnah from Abu Hanifa due to his complete care in the matter and great caution regarding it. Do you not see how the Commander of the Faithful Ali used to make the narrator take an oath? The correct view is that presumption of the handwriting in the letter and of the truthfulness in the message is sufficient: if the recipient presumes that it is the handwriting of the shaykh or if the person sent to presumes the truthfulness of the messenger in his message, it suffices, because following presumption is obligatory, unlike a judge's letter to a judge, because forgery in transactions is more than in the sunan. So a judge's letter to a judge is not accepted without witnesses. And it is valid in 'ard that the receiver says at the time of narration: haddathana, akhbarana, anba'ana, naba'ana, restricted by the reading or absolutely according to the more correct view. Al-Hakim said: On this were our imams, and he transmitted it from the four mujtahid imams. And he says in the letter and message: akhbarani, not haddathani, because 'ikhbar' is permissible when there is no face-to-face encounter, unlike 'tahdith'. Perhaps this is a convention. And the rukhsa (concession) in the first (receiving) is ijaza (permission).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines the three components of hadith transmission: tahammul (receiving), ada' (delivering), and baqa' (preserving), each having a strict form ('azima) and a concessional form (rukhsa). For tahammul, the strictest form is the shaykh reading directly from memory to the student. Abu Hanifa preferred the 'ard method (student reading back to the shaykh) when using a written copy because it ensures careful verification. This contrasts with most hadith scholars who prefer the shaykh's reading. The substitute forms include written correspondence (kitab) and oral message (risala), both considered valid if the recipient has reasonable presumption of authenticity. Abu Hanifa's requirement of witnesses for accepting a written letter was a form of caution, but the correct view is that mere presumption of handwriting is sufficient. The text also gives conventions for wording in transmission: in 'ard, one may use 'haddathana' or 'akhbarana', while in writing, only 'akhbarani' is used. The rukhsa (concession) for receiving is ijaza (permission to narrate without direct hearing).

Key Terms
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of receiving a hadith from a shaykh through recognized methods like reading, listening, or receiving a written copy
العرض (al-ard): The student reading the hadith to the shaykh, who confirms it (even tacitly)
الإجازة (al-ijaza): Permission from the shaykh to narrate a hadith without direct hearing or reading

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the preferred method of receiving hadith according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The shaykh reciting from memory
B) The student reading back (ard) from a written copy
C) Receiving a written letter from the shaykh
D) Ijaza (permission)

Key Takeaway
Hadith transmission in Hanafi usul is structured with strict and concessional forms; the 'ard method is preferred for its precision when using written sources.
