Companion Action vs. Hadith: The Hadd Dispute
Usul dispute: Can Companion action override a hadith?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position that a Companion's action may be siyasa-based (not abrogation) from the Maliki reliance on amal ahl al-Madina, and apply the principle that only khabar is a binding proof.

Core Idea
A Companion's action does not abrogate a hadith; it may be policy. Non-Sahabi action never overrides a khabar.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: can the practice of a Companion override an authentic hadith? The text uses the example of the hadd for an unmarried fornicator. We will see how Hanafi scholars explain apparent contradictions by distinguishing between legal rulings (hadd) and political expediency (siyasa). This dispute extends to the authority of non-Sahabi actions versus khabar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting evidence
The hadith of 'Ubada and Ibn Mas'ud prescribes flogging and exile for a virgin fornicator. Caliphs Ali and Umar acted differently regarding exile. Note the two opposite positions: the hadith demands exile, but the caliphs did not implement it as a hadd.
The caliphs' actions are reported as statements/actions, not as formal legal rulings.
2
Analyse the Hanafi reconciliation
The commentator explains that Ali's statement (exile as sufficient tribulation) and Umar's practice were likely based on siyasa (administrative policy), not as an abrogation of the hadd. The caliph is more knowledgeable about the implementation but may have political reasons.
Siyasa does not create a new legal ruling; the hadd remains as per khabar.
3
Extend the principle to non-Sahabi
If a non-Sahabi (even the majority of the Ummah) acts contrary to a khabar, the khabar is followed because the action of a non-Sahabi is not a proof in usul.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from the Maliki.
4
Contrast with Maliki exception
The Malikis consider the practice of the people of Madina (amal ahl al-Madina) as a form of consensus (ijma) that overrides a khabar. The Hanafis reject this, accepting only ijma of all scholars.
This is a crucial difference in usul al-fiqh.
5
State the Hanafi position and thamarah
The Hanafi position: the khabar is authoritative until proven abrogated. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi fiqh, the hadith on flogging and exile is applied; in Maliki, the practice of Madina may be given precedence.
This affects fatwa and judicial rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكرام كفى بالنفي فتنة روى الإمام محمد من طريق الإمام أبي حنيفة عن حماد عن إبراهيم قال : قال عبد الله بن مسعود في البكر يزني : يجلدان مائة وينفيان سنة. قال: قال علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله تعالى عنه : حسبهما من الفتنة أن ينفيا، وأما لفظ الكتاب فقول إبراهيم النخعي كما رواه ذلك الإمام فهذان الإمامان الهاديان المهديان عملا خلاف رواية عبادة وابن مسعود (ومثله لا يخفى عن مثلهما بل هما أولى بالعلم منهما بهذا الأمر، فإن الخليفة أحق بمعرفة أمر الحد، لأنه المأمور بالإقامة، وأما تغريب أمير المؤمنين عمر فلعله للسياسة لا لكونه حداً فافهم وتأمل فيه فإنه لا يخلو عن قلق (وإن كان هذا العامل غير صحابي ولو) كان (أكثر الأمة فالعمل بالخبر لا غير، لأن الخبر حجة وعمله ليس حجة . وليس أيضاً ثبوت الخبر مما لا يخفى عليه إلا اجتماع أهل المدينة عند المالكية فإنه إجماع وحجة ومقدم على الخبر عندهم
The honourable [Companions] considered exile as sufficient tribulation. Imam Muhammad narrated via Imam Abu Hanifa from Hammad from Ibrahim that he said: Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said regarding the unmarried fornicator: they are flogged one hundred and exiled for one year. He said: Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Exile is sufficient tribulation for them. As for the wording of the book, it is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i as narrated by that Imam. So these two guided imams acted contrary to the narration of 'Ubada and Ibn Mas'ud. And similar [rulings] are not hidden from the likes of them; rather, they are more knowledgeable of the matter than them, because the caliph is more entitled to know the matter of the hadd punishment, since he is commanded to implement it. As for the exile of Ameer al-Mu'mineen 'Umar, it was perhaps for policy, not because it is a hadd. So understand and reflect, for it is not free of unease. (And if this actor is not a Companion, even if) he is the majority of the Ummah, then action is only upon the khabar, because the khabar is a proof and his action is not a proof. Also, the establishment of the khabar is not hidden from him, except the consensus of the people of Madina according to the Malikis, for it is consensus and a proof and is given precedence over the khabar according to them.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage deals with the tension between the practice of a Companion and an apparently contrary hadith. The example is the punishment for an unmarried fornicator (al-bikr). According to the hadith of 'Ubada and Ibn Mas'ud, the punishment is flogging and exile. However, Caliph Ali and Caliph Umar are reported to have acted differently. The commentator explains that the Companions' knowledge may be superior because they implement the hadd, and their actions may be based on policy (siyasa) rather than an alternative legal ruling. The passage then extends the principle: if the person acting contrary to the khabar is not a Companion—even if it is the majority of the Ummah—the khabar is still authoritative, because their action is not a proof. The only exception is the practice of the people of Madina (amal ahl al-Madina) according to the Maliki school, which they consider binding consensus. Thus, the Hanafi position is that the action of a non-Sahabi does not override an authentic hadith.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar): A hadith report (especially ahad) that is a proof in legal rulings.
السياسة (al-siyasa): Administrative policy, not a legal ruling (hukm shari).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator suggest that the actions of Ali and Umar regarding exile do not abrogate the hadith of flogging and exile?
A) Because they were unaware of the hadith.
B) Because the exile was for policy, not as a fixed hadd.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the action of a Companion always overrides a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The action of a Companion does not automatically abrogate a hadith; it may be policy (siyasa). The khabar remains the primary proof unless abrogated.

Methods of Hadith Transmission: Tahammul, 'Ard, and Hanafi Preference
Defining the three aspects of narration and the methods of bearing hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three aspects of narration (tahammul, ada', baqa') and explain the Hanafi preference for the method of 'ard, identifying the key points of the dispute with other hadith scholars.

Core Idea
The strict form of bearing hadith is the shaykh's oral reading, but Abu Hanifa deemed 'ard (reading to the shaykh) superior for accuracy of both text and chain.

This lesson examines the definition of hadith transmission (riwaya) and its three constitutive aspects: bearing (tahammul), delivering (ada'), and preservation (baqa'). Each aspect has a strict form ('azima) and a concession (rukhsa). The central focus will be on the methods of bearing, especially the contrast between the shaykh's oral reading and the student's reading to the shaykh ('ard), and why Imam Abu Hanifa considered 'ard superior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects of narration
The passage states that narration is constituted by tahammul, ada', and baqa'. Note that each aspect has an 'azima and a rukhsa.
Tahammul is the primary focus of this lesson.
2
Define the 'azima of tahammul
The strict form is the shaykh reading from his memory to the student. According to the text, this is agreed upon as the highest method in principle.
This is the baseline for comparison.
3
Explore the method of 'ard
'Ard occurs when the student reads to the shaykh or reads from a book with the shaykh's tacit approval. Abu Hanifa preferred 'ard because it enables control of both the text (matn) and chain (sanad).
Pay attention to the commentator's reasoning: it allows careful verification.
4
Analyze the dispute and its resolution
Most hadith scholars argued that the shaykh's reading is superior, citing the Prophet's practice. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing the prophetic case from ordinary transmission. Also, note the rules for written and messenger-based transmission.
This step involves understanding the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: تتقوم الرواية فينا بـ ثلاثة (التحمل والأداء، والبقاء، وكل منها رخصة وعزيمة فالعزيمة في الأول أي لتحمل أصل وخلف، والأصل (قراءة الشيخ) عليك (من حفظه) بل التحمل به قيل هو أعلى مما عداه (اتفاقاً) وهو ظاهر (أو) قراءة الشيخ عن (كتاب وقراءتك) أيها المتحمل (أو) قراءة (غيرك عليه فيقرر ) الشيخ (ولو ظناً بأن يكون هناك قرينة تفيد ظن التقرير، وإن لم يقرر هو باللسان (وهو العرض) في الاصطلاح (ورجحه) أي العرض (أبو حنيفة) إذا كان القراءة عن كتاب لإفادته التمكن من ضبط المتن (والسند) وكمال العناية به وذهب جمع ومنهم البخاري إلى المساواة بينهما (خلافاً لأكثر المحدثين) فإنهم قالوا : قراءة الشيخ أرجح واستدلالهم بقراءة الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم على الصحابة دون قراءتهم عليه في غير محل النزاع فإنه ثمة لا يمكن القراءة من الصحابة فإن ما يوحى إليه لا يمكن المعرفة به من غير إخباره بخلاف ما نحن فيه فالفرق واضح، والخلف الكتاب والرسالة، وإليه أشار بقوله (والكتاب كالخطاب، والرسالة كالقراءة شرعاً وعرفا) فإذا كتب الشيخ حديثاً وأرسل به أو أرسل رسولاً ليقرأه على المرسل إليه وأجاز الرواية عن نفسه كفى، كما إذا أخبر مشافهة (والتعليق) أي تعليق قبول الكتاب على البينة ليشهدوا عند المكتوب إليه أنه كتاب فلان الشيخ (تضييق) في باب السنة من أبي حنيفة لكمال عنايته بأمرها وعظم احتياطه بها ألا ترى إلى أمير المؤمنين علي كيف يحلف الراوي؟ (والصحيح كفاية ظن الخط) في الكتاب (والصدق) في الرسالة، فإذا ظن المكتوب إليه أنه خط فلان الشيخ أو ظن المرسل إليه صدق الرسول في رسالته كفى لأن الاتباع بالظن واجب بخلاف كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، فإن التلبيس في المعاملات أكثر مما في السنن، فلا يقبل كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي من غير بينة ويصح في العرض أن يقول المحتمل حين الرواية حدثنا، وأخبرنا، وأنبأنا، ونبأنا مقيداً بالقراءة ومطلقاً على الأصح ، قال الحاكم على ذلك عهدنا أئمتنا، ونقله عن الأئمة الأربعة المجتهدين (و) يقول (في الكتاب والرسالة أخبرني لا حدثني، لصحة إطلاق الخبر عند عدم المشافهة دون التحديث ولعل هذا اصطلاح
Issue: Narration is constituted for us by three [aspects]: bearing, delivering, and preservation. Each of them has an 'azima (strict form) and rukhsa (concession). The 'azima in the first, i.e., bearing, is an original and a substitute. The original is: the shaykh's reading to you from his memory. Rather, bearing by this is said to be higher than others, by agreement, and it is apparent. Or: the shaykh's reading from a book, and your reading (i.e., the bearer) or another's reading to him, and the shaykh gives tacit approval, even if only by a presumption that there is a circumstance indicating presumed approval, even if he does not verbally express approval. This is 'ard in terminology. Abu Hanifa preferred 'ard when the reading is from a book, because it enables control of the text and chain and complete care. A group, including al-Bukhari, held equality between them, contrary to most hadith scholars, who said that reading of the shaykh is more preponderant. Their proof is the Prophet's (peace be upon him) reading to the Companions, not their reading to him, but this is not in the area of dispute, because there [in that case] reading from the Companions was impossible, as what is revealed to him cannot be known without his informing, unlike our case, so the difference is clear. The substitute is the book and the message. He indicated this by his saying: 'The book is like direct address, and the message is like reading, legally and customarily.' So if the shaykh writes a hadith and sends it, or sends a messenger to read it to the recipient, and permits narration from himself, it suffices, just as if he informed orally. 'And al-ta'liq' meaning suspending acceptance of the book upon testimony so that they witness to the recipient that it is the shaykh's book—this is a restriction in the chapter of sunnah from Abu Hanifa, due to his complete care for it and great precaution. Do you not see the Commander of the Faithful 'Ali how he made the narrator swear? And the correct view is that presumption of the handwriting in the book and truthfulness in the message is sufficient. So if the recipient presumes it is the shaykh's handwriting, or the recipient presumes the messenger's truthfulness in his message, it is sufficient, because following presumption is obligatory, unlike a judge's letter to another judge, because deception in transactions is more than in [hadith] reports, so a judge's letter to another judge is not accepted without testimony. And in 'ard, it is permissible for the bearer at the time of narration to say: haddathana, akhbarana, anba'ana, and naba'ana, both qualified by reading and absolute, according to the most correct view. Al-Hakim said: upon that we found our imams, and he transmitted it from the four mujtahid imams. And he says in the book and message: 'akhbarani' not 'haddathani', because it is permissible to use 'khabar' in the absence of direct address, but not 'tahdith'. Perhaps this is a convention.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on the matn of Muslim al-Thubut, explains the three foundational aspects of hadith transmission: bearing (tahammul), delivering (ada'), and preservation (baqa'). Each has a strict form ('azima) and a concession (rukhsa). The strict form of bearing is the shaykh reading to you from memory; this is agreed to be the highest method. Alternative methods include the shaykh reading from a book, or the student reading to the shaykh with his tacit approval (al-ard). Abu Hanifa considered 'ard superior when done from a book because it allows careful confirmation of both the text (matn) and chain (sanad). Most hadith scholars, however, considered the shaykh's reading as superior, citing the Prophet's practice. But the commentator argues that the prophetic situation is unique—divine revelation could only be known through the Prophet's recitation—so it does not apply to ordinary transmission. The passage also discusses written transmission (kitab) and messages (risala), equating them with oral transmission legally and customarily. There is also a discussion of ta'liq: Abu Hanifa required witnesses to verify the shaykh's handwriting for written transmissions, showing his caution. Yet, a presumption of the handwriting is sufficient, unlike judicial letters which require explicit testimony due to the higher risk of fraud in transactions. Finally, the appropriate phrasing for narrations is given: in 'ard, one may use 'haddathana', 'akhbarana', etc., while for written or message transmissions, 'akhbarani' is used, not 'haddathani'.

Key Terms
العرض (al-ard): A method of bearing where the student reads to the shaykh or reads from a book with the shaykh's tacit approval.
التحمل (al-tahammul): The first of three aspects of narration: receiving the hadith from the shaykh through approved methods.
الأداء (al-ada'): The second aspect: delivering the hadith to others after bearing it.
البقاء (al-baqa'): The third aspect: preservation of the hadith in memory or writing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is 'ard (reading from a book to the shaykh) considered preferable to the shaykh's oral reading?
A) Because it is easier for the shaykh.
B) Because it allows the student to avoid mistakes.
C) Because it enables control of both text and chain and shows complete care.
D) Because it was the method of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Understanding the methods of bearing hadith is crucial for evaluating the reliability of chains of transmission; the Hanafi school's emphasis on 'ard reflects its commitment to textual accuracy.

The Knowledge Condition in Ijaza and Munawala: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute over knowledge condition in ijaza and munawala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for the validity of ijaza and munawala, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad from Abu Yusuf on the knowledge requirement.

Core Idea
A narrator must know the contents of what he narrates — analogy to testimony on a document.

This passage discusses two concessions in hadith transmission: ijaza (authorization) and munawala (handing over). While later scholars expanded these practices, the Hanafi school imposed a critical condition: the recipient must know the contents of the transmitted material. This condition is rooted in the analogy to testimony, where a witness must know what he testifies. Within the school, Abu Yusuf took a more lenient view when the source is trustworthy, but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad maintained the stricter requirement. Understanding this dispute helps appreciate how Hanafi usul safeguards the integrity of sunna transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concessions
Recognize that ijaza is a license to narrate and munawala is the physical handover of a book. Note that both are considered rukhsah (concessions) in hadith transmission.
The text starts with 'والرخصة في الأول الإجازة' then moves to 'و الرخصة المناولة'.
2
State the Hanafi condition for validity
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require that the person authorized (mujaz) or the recipient in munawala knows the contents of what he narrates. This is likened to a witness who must know the contents of a document before testifying.
The condition is explicit: 'عند الإمامين أبي حنيفة ومحمد علم المجاز له بما أجيز به'.
3
Contrast Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf permits narration via munawala without knowledge of contents if the book is secure from alteration, analogizing to a judge's letter that is accepted without the recipient knowing its contents.
Abu Yusuf's view: 'تصح الرواية عند الأمن عن التغير ككتاب القاضي'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The text refutes Abu Yusuf's analogy by noting that a judge's letter may also contain secrets that the recipient does not know, so the analogy does not justify lack of knowledge. The stricter view prevents fraud and ensures reliability.
The rebuttal: 'كتاب القاضي وكتاب الأخبار سيان' and the point about secrets.

And the concession in the first (i.e., bearing) is ijaza: it is that the shaykh says, 'I authorize you to narrate my traditions.' The predecessors differed regarding it: some allowed it, others prohibited it. However, later scholars expanded and even allowed general ijaza to all Muslims, saying 'I authorize all Muslims' and 'all' meaning all his narrations, saying 'I authorize all my narrations'; and for the unknown, saying 'I authorize those who exist now in my lifetime'; and for the known like 'I authorize those who will be born'; and for the non-existent like 'I authorize what I will hear.' It is narrated from some tabi'un that a questioner asked for ijaza in this way, and he was amazed and said to his companions: 'This one seeks authorization to lie about me.' But the correct view is the validity of ijaza in general, due to necessity, because absolute prohibition would lead to the nullification of most sunan. For the two imams Abu Hanifa and Muhammad [al-Shaybani], it is a condition that the one authorized knows what he is authorized to narrate, contrary to what is implied by the opinion of Abu Yusuf. There is no dispute regarding the validity of ijaza for blessing (tabarruk) according to the shaykh's wording; the dispute is only regarding its validity for the work of the mujtahid. And the concession is munawala: it is that the shaykh hands the book to the listener and says, 'These are my hadith from so-and-so,' or the listener hands [it] to the shaykh and says, 'These are my hadith from you or from...' and he [the shaykh] gives tacit approval. Sometimes ijaza accompanies it, sometimes not; there is a general-specific relationship between them. He said: 'It is more specific than ijaza in one respect.' According to the Hanafis, if the authorized person knows what is in the book, narration is permissible for him, like testimony on a document: if the witness knows what is in the document, he may testify. If he does not know what is in the book, then if the book is susceptible to alteration (by being with someone not trustworthy), narration is not valid at all due to doubt. And if it is not susceptible to alteration, then similarly narration is not valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, who validates narration when secure from alteration, like a judge's letter to another judge. Knowledge of the witnesses of its contents is a condition according to the two [imams], so it is not accepted when the witnesses do not know its contents, even if it is secure from alteration, contrary to him. So according to this, the hadith book that is munawal... and the saying of Shams al-A'imma that the invalidity due to lack of knowledge in narration is agreed upon among our three imams, and Abu Yusuf's permission in the book is due to necessity of including secrets hidden from others; if it were not accepted without knowledge, the purpose would be lost. Unlike hadith books, they do not contain secrets and concealment is not intended. This is refuted by the fact that [containing secrets] is in public books, not in the court document, so the judge's book and the hadith book are similar. There is something in it: indeed the judge may ask another judge or inform about hidden matters as well. And sometimes he writes in the sent letter his secrets and his ruling together. Then the preferable [formula] in both (i.e., ijaza and munawala) is 'ajazani'; it is also permissible to say 'akhbarani' and 'haddathani' qualified by ijaza and absolutely, according to the most correct school, contrary to some. And 'haddathani' is allowed due to direct address, because there is direct address in it. And wujada: it is that the student finds a book in the shaykh's handwriting, like a will to narrate for the student. And i'lam: it is that the shaykh informs that what is in this book is from his narrations from so-and-so, but he did not hand it over nor authorize it. This is not free from soundness. As for the absolute use of 'haddathani' and 'akhbarani' in these, it is a weak hadith due to the absence of informing and narrating, unless they have agreed upon a more general meaning. And the 'azima in the second, which is preservation, is continuous memorization until the time of delivery; and the rukhsa is to recall by looking at the book what is in it, even if he does not remember what is in it, and he knows that it is from...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the concessions (rukhsa) in the bearing of hadith. The first rukhsa is ijaza: the shaykh giving general or specific permission to narrate. The early scholars differed, but later scholars, due to necessity (darura), allowed ijaza even in general terms. For Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, a condition is that the recipient knows the contents of what he is authorized to narrate. Abu Yusuf is more lenient. The second rukhsa is munawala: physical handover of a book with permission. The Hanafis require that the recipient knows the contents, like a witness must know the contents of a document. If the book is vulnerable to alteration, narration is invalid. If it is secure, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad still require knowledge; Abu Yusuf allows it if secure, analogizing to a judge's letter. The text then defends the stricter view, arguing that even judge's letters can contain secrets, so the analogy stands. Then it discusses the preferred phrasing for narrating by ijaza and munawala: 'ajazani' is best; 'akhbarani' and 'haddathani' are also allowed when qualified. Finally, it introduces wujada (finding a book) and i'lam (notification), noting they have some validity. The passage ends with a return to the second aspect (preservation), explaining the 'azima of memorization and the rukhsa of looking at the book even if one does not remember but knows it is from the shaykh.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijaza): A license from the shaykh to narrate his hadith, which may be general or specific.
المناولة (al-munawala): A method where the shaykh physically hands over a book of hadith to the student, often with or without explicit ijaza.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad for the validity of munawala?
A) The shaykh must read the book aloud.
B) The recipient must know what is in the book.
C) The book must be sealed.
D) The shaykh must be present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that a narrator knows the contents of what he narrates, even when authorized by ijaza or munawala, to preserve the integrity of prophetic traditions.

Ijaza and Munawala: Conditions for Valid Narration
Analyzing the valid forms of ijaza and munawala and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions for the validity of ijaza and munawala and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of knowledge of contents.

Core Idea
Ijaza and munawala require the recipient to know the contents; otherwise, narration is invalid.

This passage examines the forms of ijaza and munawala and the debated condition of knowledge. You will learn why later Hanafis permitted ijaza out of necessity, and why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad insisted on knowledge of contents, while Abu Yusuf permitted it under assurance of security. The discussion uses analogies to testimony and judge's letters.

Guided Path
1
Identify types of ijaza
List the various types of ijaza mentioned: general, specific, for all Muslims, for known/unknown persons. Note that later scholars expanded its application due to necessity.
2
Locate the dispute on knowledge condition
Determine the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: knowledge of contents is required. Contrast with Abu Yusuf's view that it is not required when the book is secure.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the analogies
Compare the analogy to testimony on a document (requires knowledge) and the analogy to a judge's letter (accepted even without knowledge according to Abu Yusuf). Understand the Hanafi response that the judge's letter is a special case due to secrecy.
The text repels the judge's letter analogy by noting that even in judge's letters, witnesses must know the contents.
4
Apply to munawala
For munawala (handing over a book), the same condition applies: if the recipient knows the contents, narration is valid like testimony; if not, the ruling depends on trustworthiness of the book.
If the book is susceptible to alteration, narration is invalid; if not, still invalid according to the majority.
5
Determine recommended wording
Note the recommended formulas: 'ajazani' for both ijaza and munawala; 'akhbarani' and 'haddathani' are permissible with or without qualification by ijaza, though 'haddathani' is only for oral transmission.

The second principle/ The Sunnah. Fulan, and it is that the shaykh says: 'I have granted you permission to narrate my narrations.' And the early scholars differed concerning it; some permitted it and some prohibited it. But the later scholars expanded even to permit a general permission for all Muslims, i.e., by saying 'I grant permission to all Muslims.' And 'by all' means all his narrations, by saying 'I grant permission for all my narrations.' And 'for the unknown' means by saying 'I grant permission to whoever is present now in my life.' And 'for the known' such as 'I grant permission to whoever will be born.' And 'for the non-existent' such as 'I grant permission for what I will hear.' And it is reported from some of the Successors that a questioner asked for permission in this manner, so he was amazed and said to his companions: 'This one seeks permission to lie about me.' The soundest view is that permission is valid in general, out of necessity, because absolute prohibition leads to the loss of most sunan, for that which is conditional according to the two Imams Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the permission-grantee's knowledge of what he is permitted to narrate, contrary to what follows from the analogy of Abu Yusuf's statement. There is no dispute about the validity of the permission for blessing, by the tongue of the shaykh; the dispute is only about its validity for the action of the mujtahid. And the concession called munawala: it is that the shaykh hands the book to the listener and says: 'These are my hadith from so-and-so,' or the listener hands to the shaykh and says: 'These are my hadith from you,' or it is ratified. Sometimes it is accompanied by permission and sometimes not, so between them is a general-special relationship as he said: it is more specific than permission in one aspect. According to the Hanafis, if the permission-grantee knows what is in the book, narration is permissible for him, like bearing witness to a document: the witness, if he knows what is in the document, may testify. Otherwise, if he does not know what is in the book: if the book is susceptible to alteration, i.e., it is with someone not trustworthy, narration is not valid at all due to doubt. And if it is not susceptible to alteration, similarly narration is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for he validates narration when there is assurance against alteration, like a judge's letter to a judge. For knowledge of the witnesses of its contents is a condition according to them, so it is not accepted when the witnesses lack knowledge of its contents even if it is assured against alteration, contrary to him. According to this, a book of hadith given by munawala — and Shams al-A'imma's statement that invalidity without knowledge in narration is agreed upon by our three imams, and Abu Yusuf's permission in the case of the book is due to necessity of its containing secrets hidden from other than the addressee; if it were not accepted without knowledge, the purpose would be lost. Unlike books of reports, which do not contain secrets nor aim to conceal them. This is repelled by the fact that containing secrets in books is in public books, not in the judge's record book. So the judge's book and the book of reports are equal, and there is what is in it, because a judge may ask another judge or inform hidden matters as well. And perhaps in the sent letter he writes his secrets and his ruling together. Then the recommended in both, i.e., permission and munawala, is 'ajazani' (he permitted me), and 'akhbarani' and 'haddathani' are permissible, restricted to permission, and unrestricted, according to the soundest madhhab, contrary to some. And 'haddathani' is allowed only for oral address, i.e., because oral address is present in it.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of ijaza (license to narrate) and munawala (handing over) in hadith transmission. It begins by noting that early scholars differed on ijaza, but later scholars generally permitted it, including general ijaza for all Muslims, for all narrations, and for known/unknown persons. The text states that the correct view is its validity in general due to necessity, as total prohibition would lead to the loss of many sunan. A crucial condition according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that the recipient must know the contents of what he is licensed to narrate; Abu Yusuf differs but is overruled by necessity arguments in certain cases. The passage then defines munawala and compares it to ijaza, noting they overlap. For hearing via munawala, if the recipient knows the contents, narration is valid like testifying to a document; if he does not, the ruling depends on the book's trustworthiness. The Hanafis require knowledge of contents, unlike Abu Yusuf who validates in cases of security, drawing an analogy with a judge's letter. The discussion ends with the recommended formula: 'ajazani' for both ijaza and munawala, and the permissibility of 'akhbarani' and 'haddathani' with or without qualification by ijaza, though 'haddathani' is only for oral transmission.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijāza): License granted by a shaykh to a student to narrate his hadith. It can be general for all Muslims or specific.
المناولة (al-munāwala): Handing over a book by the shaykh to the student, saying 'These are my hadith from so-and-so.' It may be accompanied by ijaza or not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is required for a person receiving ijaza to validly narrate from the book?
A) The ijaza must be oral.
B) The recipient must know the contents of the book.
C) The book must be in the handwriting of the shaykh.
D) The ijaza must be general for all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The validity of ijaza and munawala hinges on the recipient's knowledge of the contents; this condition protects the integrity of hadith transmission.

Narrating from Handwriting: Wujada and the Hanafi Caution
Comparing wujada and i'lam to ijaza and munawala, and debating validity of narration by recognizing handwriting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi usul position on the validity of wujada (narration from handwriting) and apply the conditions for its acceptance.

Core Idea
Narrating from a shaykh's handwriting without memory is accepted by most usulis, but Abu Hanifa's caution demands memory for certainty.

This lesson examines the usul debate over wujada (finding a book in a shaykh's handwriting) and i'lam (the shaykh's verbal notice). The central question: can a hadith scholar narrate from a book by merely recognizing the handwriting, without remembering the contents? We'll explore the positions of the majority and the cautious Hanafi view, and evaluate the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Define wujada and i'lam
Understand the technical meanings: wujada is finding a book in the shaykh's handwriting; i'lam is the shaykh informing the student without handing over or ijaza. Both are considered weak forms of transmission.
The text notes that using 'haddathani' or 'akhbarani' in these cases is weak unless a convention is agreed.
2
Identify the core disputed ruling
The main disagreement: is it valid to narrate a hadith from a book by only recognizing the shaykh's handwriting, without remembering the contents? Most usul scholars accept it, while Abu Hanifa requires memory.
Abu Hanifa's caution is extreme: he rejected even ijaza absolutely and only accepted handwriting with memory.
3
Examine the majority's primary proof
The majority cite the Companions' practice with the Prophet's letters, especially the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm. They argue that the Companions relied on handwriting recognition alone, establishing the principle.
The book of 'Amr ibn Hazm is considered highly authentic by Ya'qub ibn Sufyan.
4
Apply the Hanafi response (naqd)
The Hanafi rebuttal is that this is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). In those cases, the Companions knew the contents or there were decisive contextual clues (qara'in) producing certainty, not mere assumption.
The text warns not to slip on this point: the existence of qarina may cut off the analogy.
5
Determine the chosen view and its practical implication
The text states the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that of the majority, but it also highlights Abu Hanifa's caution. Practically, a mufti should require either memory or a strong qarina for narration from handwriting.
Abu Hanifa's narrations are few because his conditions are rarely met.

والتابعين يرجعون إليه ويدعون آراءهم وككتاب أمير المؤمنين الصديق الأكبر رضي الله عنه، وقال الزهري عن سالم عن أبيه أنه كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم قد كتب الصدقة ولم يخرجها إلى عماله. وتوفي فأخرجها أبو بكر من بعده، فعمل بها حتى ،قبض، ثم أخرجها عمر فعمل بها حتى قبض . ثم أخرجها عثمان فعمل بها حتى ،قبض، ثم أخرجها علي فعمل بها (أقول) هذا (قياس مع الفارق) فإن الكلام في أنه هل يصح رواية معنى الحديث بعيد جداً هذا ، اعلم أن الإمام أبا حنيفة احتاط في باب السنة جداً، فمنع الكتاب والرسالة إلا بالبينة ولم يعتمد على الرسول (ومنع الإجازة مطلقاً، ولم يعمل بالخط إلا متذكراً، ولهذا قلت الروايات عنه فإن اجتماع هذه الشرائط، قلما وذلك لأن السنة أصل الدين كالكتاب وفيها وإن لم يجب التواتر) للضرورة (لكن إرخاء عنان التوسعة) فيها (مطلقاً تأسيس للتعارض والتشاجر) فإنه لو اعتبرت بجميع أنحائها وقع
And wujada: it is that the student finds a book in the handwriting of the shaykh, like a bequest of narration for the student. And i'lam: it is that the shaykh informs [the student] that what is in this book is from my narrations from so-and-so, but he did not hand it over nor grant permission. Both are not devoid of validity. As for using 'haddathani' and 'akhbarani' in these two, it is a weak hadith due to lack of informing and narrating, unless they agree upon a more general meaning. The resolution in the second is constancy: continued memory until the time of performance, heart evidence; the concession is to remember after looking at the book what is in it, even if he does not remember what is in it, and he knows that it is from [قَالَ: حنيفة?] and it is sound according to most of the usul scholars, and it is the chosen view. On this disagreement: the witness seeing his handwriting on a document, so testimony is permitted upon knowing his handwriting even if he does not remember its contents according to most, contrary to him. And the judge seeing his handwriting in the record book, so he does not permit acting upon it, contrary to the majority. And it is reported from Abu Yusuf permissibility in narration and in the record book because they are trustworthy, unlike the document because it is in the hands of litigants so there is no trust. And it is reported from Imam Muhammad in all cases, whether narration, or record book, or document, as a facilitation for us. As I say: knowing his handwriting while the book is in his hand or the hand of a trustworthy person produces the assumption (zann) that it is his hearing and his writing, or the hearing of a trustworthy person and his writing; and lack of memory is not a hindrance to this assumption out of necessity, and following assumption is obligatory, so it must be accepted. You might say: that it produces assumption is denied; rather the custom with books of hadith is memory and scrutiny to derive their meanings and what is in them; if he does not remember, it is possible that he was careless in memory and accuracy, so it does not produce assumption. So ponder over it, unlike the transmission of the Qur'an, for it is often memorized for blessing in the writing itself, so understand. And he argued first with the practice of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, by his (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) letter, by knowing the handwriting and knowing that it is attributed to him (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions), like the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm, recorded by al-Hakim, which contains the amounts of zakat and blood-money; and al-Nasa'i recorded it in the Book of Blood-Money. Ya'qub ibn Sufyan said: We do not know among all transmitted books a more authentic one than this, for the Companions of the Prophet (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) and the Successors refer to it and abandon their own opinions. And like the letter of the Commander of the Faithful, al-Siddiq al-Akbar (may Allah be pleased with him). Al-Zuhri reported from Salim from his father that the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) had written the charity [rules] but did not send it out to his agents. He died, so Abu Bakr took it out after him and acted upon it until he died, then 'Umar took it out and acted upon it until he died, then 'Uthman took it out and acted upon it until he died, then 'Ali took it out and acted upon it. I say: This is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). For the discussion is whether it is valid to narrate what is in the book by finding it in the handwriting of a trustworthy person without remembering it, and it is not about narrating the book itself. So where is this from that? And the analogy of the first upon the second is not valid because it is possible that the family of 'Amr ibn Hazm were narrators of the book and knew what was in it. So understand and do not slip on that a contextual clue may produce certainty, and it is possible that here there is a decisive clue indicating that the book is the book of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions). So ponder over it, for it is a remote probability contrary to the apparent. And he argued secondly that forgetfulness is prevalent; man is prone to oversight and forgetfulness; so if memory were required in narrating what is in the book, many proofs would be invalidated due to forgetfulness. He answered: that prevalence after knowing the handwriting is denied, and the discussion is about that forgetfulness among those who deal with hadith in their notebooks and not understanding the meaning of hadith is very remote. Know that Imam Abu Hanifa exercised extreme caution in the chapter of the Sunnah, so he prohibited [accepting] a book or letter without clear evidence and did not rely on the messenger; and he prohibited ijaza absolutely, and did not act upon handwriting except with memory. For this reason the narrations from him are few, because these conditions rarely come together. This is because the Sunnah is the foundation of the religion like the Book, and although it does not require tawatur due to necessity, loosening the reins of leniency absolutely establishes conflict and dispute.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of wujada (finding a book in the shaykh's handwriting) and i'lam (the shaykh's verbal confirmation) for narration. It notes these methods are not devoid of validity but the use of 'haddathani' and 'akhbarani' in these contexts is considered weak except by convention. The main issue is whether one can narrate from a book by recognizing the handwriting without remembering the contents. Most usul scholars accept this based on the assumption (zann) produced by knowing the handwriting, following the principle of necessity. A comparison is made with testimony on a document: the majority allow testifying upon seeing one's handwriting even without memory, while there is disagreement about a judge's record book. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad offer different views. The passage then presents two proofs for acceptance: first, the practice of the Companions relying on the Prophet's letters (e.g., the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm) by recognizing the handwriting, and second, the prevalence of forgetfulness which would invalidate many proofs if memory were required. These proofs are rebutted: the first is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq) because in those cases the Companions knew the contents; the second is countered by saying that forgetfulness is not prevalent among careful hadith scholars. The passage concludes that Imam Abu Hanifa was extremely cautious: he required either a clear chain or memory of contents, thus his narrations are few because these conditions are rare. The reason is that the Sunnah is a foundational source like the Qur'an; excessive leniency would lead to conflict.

Key Terms
الوجادة (al-wijāda): Finding a book in the handwriting of a shaykh, considered a valid means of transmission by many.
الإعلام (al-i‘lām): The shaykh informing the student that the book contains his narrations, without handing it over or granting permission.
قرينة (qarīna): A contextual clue that may produce certainty about the authenticity of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the majority of usul scholars accept narrating from a book by recognizing handwriting without remembering the contents?
A) Because the handwriting produces certainty (qat').
B) Because forgetting is impossible for scholars.
C) Because knowing the handwriting produces a valid assumption (zann) and necessity requires accepting it.
D) Because Abu Hanifa himself permitted it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul position on wujada balances caution and facilitation: the majority accept narration from handwriting without memory, while Abu Hanifa's strict requirement of memory reflects his broader caution in the Sunnah.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
15 min
The Dispute on Acting upon Handwriting without Memory
Dispute over accepting written records in hadith narration without recollection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on acting upon handwriting without memory, explain the majority counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Handwriting gives presumption, but Abu Hanifa required memory to protect the Sunnah from forgery.

A core usul question: if you recognize your own handwriting or that of a trustworthy person, can you narrate the content without recalling it? This dispute between Abu Hanifa and the majority of usul scholars has practical implications for hadith transmission and judicial testimony. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling, the proofs, and the rationale behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul issue
Determine the exact act being ruled upon: accepting a written report (narration or testimony) based solely on recognizing the handwriting of a trustworthy person without any memory of the content.
The issue is about the evidentiary value of handwriting (khatt) when recollection (tadhakkur) is absent.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa forbids narration or action merely on handwriting recognition; memory of the content is required. This applies to a witness seeing his handwriting in a document (sakk) and a judge seeing his in a record (sijill).
His caution aims to preserve the Sunnah from forgery and laxity.
3
State the majority position
The majority (including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allows acting upon handwriting recognition when the document is in the person's own hand or that of a trustworthy person, because recognition generates a valid presumption (zann) that the content is authentic.
Imam Muhammad extended this to all cases – narration, sijill, and sakk.
4
Analyze the first proof (companion practice)
The majority cites the Companions' use of the Prophet's letters (e.g., letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm) by recognizing handwriting and attribution. The Hanafi response argues this is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): the Companions knew the contents, so they did not rely on handwriting alone without memory.
The proof is zanni in transmission and meaning.
5
Analyze the second proof (forgetfulness)
The majority argues that forgetfulness is common; requiring memory would invalidate many proofs. The Hanafi response denies that forgetfulness is prevalent among hadith scholars who engage with meaning, and argues that handwriting alone does not overcome the possibility of laxity.
This response challenges the necessity argument.
6
Evaluate the tarjih
The author (Ibn Abd al-Shakur) declares the majority view as the chosen one (al-mukhtar), but acknowledges Abu Hanifa's caution as a legitimate ikhtilaf. The practical fatwa today follows the majority, though Abu Hanifa's view is respected as a stringent safeguard.
Understanding both views is essential for nuanced fiqh.

عند الأكثر خلافاً له ورؤية القاضي خطه في السجل فلا يجوز عنده العمل به خلافاً للأكثر وروي عن أبي يوسف الجواز في الرواية والسجل لأنهما مأمونان دون الصك لأنه في أيدي الخصوم فلا أمان وروي عن الإمام محمد في الكل رواية كان أو سجلاً أو صكاً تيسيراً لنا كما أقول معرفة خطه وهو في يده أو في يد ثقة تقتضي الظن بكونه مسموعه ومكتوبه أو مسموع ثقة مكتوبه وعدم التذكر ليس بمانع عن هذا الظن ضرورة واتباع الظن واجب كثيراً ما يحفظ للتبرك بنفس المكتوب فافهم واستدل أولاً بعمل الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم بكتابه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام بمعرفة الخط ومعرفة أنه منسوب إليه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام ككتاب عمرو بن حزم أخرجه الحاكم وهو مشتمل على مقادير الزكاة والديات وأخرجه النسائي في الديات قال يعقوب بن سفيان لا نعلم في جميع الكتب المنقولة أصح منه فإن أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم والتابعين يرجعون إليه ويدعون آراءهم وككتاب أمير المؤمنين الصديق الأكبر رضي الله عنه وقال الزهري عن سالم عن أبيه أنه كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم قد كتب الصدقة ولم يخرجها إلى عماله وتوفي فأخرجها أبو بكر من بعده فعمل بها حتى قبض ثم أخرجها عمر فعمل بها حتى قبض ثم أخرجها عثمان فعمل بها حتى قبض ثم أخرجها علي فعمل بها أقول هذا قياس مع الفارق فإن الكلام في أنه هل يصح رواية ما في الكتاب بوجدانه بخط الثقة من غير تذكر به وليس في رواية الكتاب فأين هذا من ذلك وقياس الأول على الثاني لا يصح لجواز أن يكون آل عمرو بن حزم راوي الكتاب وعالمين ما فيه فافهم ولا تزل على أن القرينة قد تفيد القطع ويجوز أن يكون ههنا قرينة قاطعة دالة على أن الكتاب كتاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فتأمل فيه فإنه احتمال بعيد خلاف الظاهر واستدل ثانياً النسيان غالب فإن الإنسان يسارق السهو والنسيان فلو لزم في رواية ما في الكتاب التذكر بطل كثير من الأدلة بالنسيان وأجيب بأن الغلبة بعد معرفة الخط ممنوع والكلام فيه على أن النسيان في المتصدين للحديث بما في خرائطهم وعدم فهم معنى الحديث بعيد جداً هذا اعلم أن الإمام أبا حنيفة احتاط في باب السنة جداً فمنع الكتاب والرسالة إلا بالبينة ولم يعتمد على الرسول ومنع الإجازة مطلقاً ولم يعمل بالخط إلا متذكراً ولهذا قلت الروايات عنه فإن اجتماع هذه الشرائط قلما وذلك لأن السنة أصل الدين كالكتاب وفيها وإن لم يجب التواتر للضرورة لكن إرخاء عنان التوسعة فيها مطلقاً تأسيس للتعارض والتشاجر فإنه لو اعتبرت بجميع أنحائها وقع التعارض كثيراً وفتح الباب التقصير في حفظ الحديث والبدعة فإن الاعتماد على الخط يؤدي إلى قبول كل مكتوب والتلبيس فيه ممكن بل واقع فينفتح البدعة ألا ترى إلى تحليف أمير المؤمنين علي كيف احتاط هذا اعلم أن علم ما في الكتاب ومعرفة المعنى إنما شرطهما الإمام لأن المقصود في السنة المعنى ولا يتصدى لها في العادة إلا لمعرفة المعاني وأخذ الأحكام ومن قصر فيها يكون متساهلاً فيه والمتصدى للسنة قلما ينسى ما كتب عنده في خرائطه فافهم
His handwriting or the handwriting of a trustworthy person other than him, and it (i.e., the book) is in his hand or in the hand of a trustworthy person, narration is forbidden. The ruling with Abu Hanifa? According to the majority of usul scholars, and it is the chosen view. On this difference is the witness seeing his handwriting in the document (sakk), so testimony is permitted upon recognizing his handwriting even if not remembering its content according to the majority, contrary to him. And the judge seeing his handwriting in the record (sijill), so acting upon it is not permitted according to him, contrary to the majority. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf the permissibility in narration and the record because both are trustworthy, unlike the document because it is in the hands of opponents so no trust. And it is narrated from Imam Muhammad in all cases—whether narration, record, or document—as a facilitation for us. I say: Recognizing his handwriting while it is in his hand or in the hand of a trustworthy person gives rise to the presumption that it is his heard and written, or heard from a trustworthy person written by him. And lack of remembrance is not an obstacle to this presumption out of necessity, and following presumption is obligatory, so acceptance is obligatory. You might say: That it creates presumption is denied; rather, the custom with hadith books is memorization and examination for deriving their meanings and what is in them. So if he does not remember, it is possible that he was lax in memorization and precision, so it does not yield presumption. So ponder on it, unlike the transmission of the Qur'an, which is often memorized for blessing by the writing itself, so understand. And he argued firstly by the practice of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, with his book, peace be upon him and his family and companions, by recognizing the handwriting and knowing that it is attributed to him, like the book of Amr ibn Hazm, transmitted by al-Hakim, which includes the amounts of zakat and blood-money; and al-Nasa'i transmitted it in the chapter of blood-money. Ya'qub ibn Sufyan said: We do not know among all transmitted books a more authentic one than it, for the Companions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the Successors refer to it and abandon their opinions. And like the book of the Commander of the Faithful, al-Siddiq al-Akbar, may Allah be pleased with him. Al-Zuhri said from Salim from his father that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, had written the sadaqa but did not send it out to his officials. He died, so Abu Bakr sent it out after him and acted upon it until he passed away, then 'Umar sent it out and acted upon it until he passed away, then 'Uthman sent it out and acted upon it until he passed away, then 'Ali sent it out and acted upon it. I say: This is an analogy with a difference. For the discussion is about whether it is valid to narrate what is in the book by finding it in the handwriting of a trustworthy person without remembering it, and this is not about narrating the book. So where is this from that? And the analogy of the first on the second is invalid because it is possible that the family of 'Amr ibn Hazm were narrators of the book and familiar with its contents. So understand, and do not slip from that the circumstantial evidence may yield certainty. And it is possible that here there is a definitive circumstantial evidence indicating that the book is the book of the Messenger of Allah. So ponder on it, for it is a remote possibility contrary to the apparent. And he argued secondly: Forgetfulness is prevalent, for man is subject to forgetfulness. So if remembering were required in narrating what is in the book, many proofs would be invalidated due to forgetfulness. And it was answered: The prevalence after recognizing handwriting is denied, and the discussion is about that forgetfulness among those engaged in hadith with what is in their bundles and not understanding the meaning of the hadith is very far-fetched. Know that Imam Abu Hanifa was extremely cautious in the chapter of the Sunnah. So he prohibited [accepting] the book and letter except with evidence, and did not rely on the messenger, and he prohibited the licence (ijaza) absolutely, and did not act upon handwriting except with memory. Hence narrations from him are few, because these conditions are rarely met. And that is because the Sunnah is the foundation of the religion like the Book, and although it does not require tawatur out of necessity, but slackening the reins of expansion absolutely establishes conflict and discord. For if it were considered in all its aspects, conflict would occur frequently, and it opens the door to negligence in preserving hadith and innovation. For reliance on handwriting leads to accepting every written thing, and tampering is possible, indeed it has occurred, so innovation would open. Do you not see the swearing of the Commander of the Faithful 'Ali, how cautious he was. Know that knowledge of what is in the book and understanding the meaning are required by the imam, because the purpose in the Sunnah is meaning, and one does not generally engage in it except for understanding meanings and deriving rulings. And one who falls short in that is negligent. And one engaged in the Sunnah rarely forgets what he has written in his bundles. So understand.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage discusses a critical usul issue: Is it permissible to narrate a hadith or act upon a written document (like a judge's record or a letter) based solely on recognizing the handwriting of a trustworthy person, without actually remembering hearing the content? The passage presents the Hanafi position and the views of other scholars. The text begins by stating the ruling: if a book is in one's own hand or that of a trustworthy person, but the person does not recall the content, narration (or acting upon it) is forbidden according to Abu Hanifa. However, the majority of usul scholars allow it, and the author indicates that this majority view is the chosen position. The basis for disagreement is seen in two applications: (1) A witness sees his own handwriting on a document (sakk) – according to the majority, he can testify based on recognizing his handwriting even if he does not remember the content; Abu Hanifa disagrees. (2) A judge sees his handwriting in a record (sijill) – Abu Hanifa does not allow him to act upon it without memory, while the majority do. Then the text narrates that Abu Yusuf allowed it in narration and records (sijill) because they are in the hands of trusted officials, unlike documents in the hands of litigants. Imam Muhammad allowed it in all cases for convenience. The author then presents the reasoning: recognizing the handwriting in one's own possession or a trustworthy person's hand gives a presumption (zann) that it is the same as what was heard or written by a trustworthy person. Lack of memory is not an obstacle because acting on such presumption is obligatory. A potential objection is raised: does handwriting really generate presumption? After all, hadith scholars typically memorize and examine for meaning, so if they forget, it suggests carelessness. The author responds that there is a difference with Quranic transmission, where preservation is for blessing itself. Then the passage gives proofs: first, the practice of the Companions who acted upon the Prophet's letters by recognizing handwriting and attribution, e.g., the letter of Amr ibn Hazm about zakat and blood-money, which was used by later caliphs. The author counters that this is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq), because the Companions knew the contents of the letter and its context, so they were not relying on handwriting alone without memory. Second, the argument that forgetfulness is common and requiring memory would invalidate many proofs. The response is that forgetfulness among hadith scholars who engage with the content is unlikely, and the claim of prevalence is denied. Finally, the author explains Imam Abu Hanifa's caution: he required high standards to protect the Sunnah from conflict, negligence, and innovation. Reliance on writing without memory could open the door to accepting forgeries. The practice of Imam 'Ali in making witnesses swear is cited as an example. The key takeaway: the majority view is that recognizing handwriting suffices for the presumption needed for acceptance, but Abu Hanifa's stricter position is a cautionary measure to preserve the integrity of the Sunnah.

Key Terms
الخط (al-khatt): Handwriting; the physical script that can be recognized as belonging to a particular person.
التذكر (al-tadhakkur): Remembering the content of a written report; having conscious recollection of the information.
الصك (al-sakk): A legal document or deed, usually in the hands of litigants.
السجل (al-sijill): A judge's official record; a document kept in the court or with a trusted official.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding acting upon a written document if one recognizes the handwriting but does not remember the content?
A) It is permissible because handwriting recognition gives a valid presumption.
B) It is impermissible unless one remembers the content or has clear memory.
C) It is permissible only for judges' records (sijill) but not for documents (sakk).
D) It is permissible only if the document is in the possession of a trustworthy person.

Key Takeaway
Handwriting recognition yields a valid presumption (zann) for accepting a report, but Abu Hanifa requires additional memory to safeguard the Sunnah from forgery.

Narrating Hadith by Meaning: Permissibility and the Five Categories
Classification of hadith texts and rules for narrating by meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Fakhr al-Islam's five-category classification to determine whether narrating a given hadith by meaning is permissible, and for whom.

Core Idea
Narrating by meaning is a rukhsa for qualified scholars; for ambiguous texts it is forbidden.

The accurate transmission of prophetic hadith is a foundational concern of usul al-fiqh. While the ideal (azima) is to transmit the exact wording, the Hanafi school permits narrating by meaning (al-naql bi al-ma'na) under specific conditions. This lesson unpacks the detailed classification by Imam Fakhr al-Islam, which categorizes hadith texts based on clarity and interpretability, and specifies who may paraphrase each type. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for evaluating the reliability of hadith transmissions and for proper istinbat (derivation of rulings).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish azima and rukhsa
Identify that verbatim transmission (azima) is the preferred method, while narrating by meaning (rukhsa) is a permissible concession only for those who know the language and are well-versed in the Shari'a.
The passage emphasizes that azima is superior but rukhsa is allowed under conditions.
2
Understand the dual condition of language and fiqh
Recognize that the required qualification depends on the hadith's content: if the hadith deals with linguistic meanings, knowledge of Arabic suffices; if it deals with legal meanings, juristic understanding (fiqh) is also necessary.
This nuance refines the general statement that one must be 'knowledgeable in language and Shari'a'.
3
Memorize Fakhr al-Islam's five categories
Learn the five categories: (1) mufassar/muhkam – perfectly clear; (2) nass/zahir – apparent but interpretable; (3) mushkil/mushtarak – requiring interpretation; (4) mujmal (before bayan) and mutashabih – unknown meaning; (5) jawami' al-kalim – the Prophet's comprehensive speech.
Each category has a different ruling on narrating by meaning.
4
Apply the permissibility rule to each category
For category 1: anyone knowing Arabic. For category 2: only a jurist. For categories 3, 4, and 5: narrating by meaning is not permissible.
Category 4 (mujmal after bayan) becomes like category 1 or 2.
5
Recognize the acceptance of such narrations
Note that even if narration by meaning is allowed, the narration is accepted (maqbul) from upright narrators, with the understanding that the narrator is trustworthy and will not distort the meaning.
The passage ends with an important note about qabul (acceptance) being undisputed.

والعزيمة في الثالث أي الأداء اللفظ المسموع من في رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أو في شيخه والرخصة جواز النقل بالمعنى للعالم باللغة المتفقة بالشريعة الظاهر أنه يشترط للنقل بالمعنى التفقه بالشريعة وليس مختار المصنف فينبغي أن يحمل على أنه يجب في النقل بالمعنى العلم باللغة إن كان الحديث وارداً على المعاني اللغوية والتفقه في الشريعة إن كان وارداً على المعنى الشرعي نعم الأولى النقل بصورته للعمل به كالمشكل والمشترك وما لا يدرك بالتأويل يحتاج إلى السماع كالمجمل أو لا يدرك أصلاً كالمتشابه وجوامع الكلم فالأول يجوز نقله بالمعنى لكل عارف باللغة إذ لا احتمال للغلط في فهم المعنى لعدم قبوله التأويل والتخصيص أصلاً وأما الثاني فلا يجوز إلا لفقيه فإنه يجوز أن يقيم غير الفقيه بدله لفظاً لا يحتمل ذلك التأويل ويكون هو مراد الشارع فيفوت الحكم وأما الفقيه فيعرف حق كل لفظ فلا يغير بحيث ينقلب من الظهور إلى الأحكام وأما الثالث فلا يحل فيه النقل بالمعنى أصلاً لأن المعنى لا يفهم فيه إلا بتأويل واستعمال رأي والرائي يخطىء ويصيب فما هو غير واجب الاتباع يصير واجب الاتباع بالنسبة إلى المعصوم صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه فإن قلت لعله يعرف بالقرينة قلنا لو كانت القرينة مقرونة بحيث تجعله متضح المعنى لغة فيدخل في الأولين وأما الرابع فلا يحتمل نقل المعنى فيه فإن المتشابه لا يعرف معناه وأما المجمل فقبل سماع البيان مثل المتشابه وبعده النقل نقل المجمل والبيان وهما حديثان متضحا المعنى وأما الخامس فلان جوامع الكلم مخصوص بها إعطاء رسولنا صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كما يدل عليه الخبر الصحيح ولا يمكن إتيان مثله فلو نقل بمعناه نقل على فهمه وعلى ما يتأدى من عبارته فيفوت أكثر الفوائد المشتملة هي عليها ثم هذا قوله في جواز النقل وأما القبول فلا نزاع فيه ويقبل مطلقاً ويحمل على أن ما نقله الراوي من صور يجوز نقله بالمعنى لكونه عدلاً لا يرتكب المحذور ولا ينسب إلى الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ما فيه ريبة كيف وإذا نقل بالمعنى لم يعلم اللفظ المسموع فكيف
And the determined form (azima) in the third is performance with the heard wording from the Messenger of Allah or from his teacher, and the concession (rukhsa) is the permissibility of narrating by meaning for one who knows the language and is well-versed in the Shari'a. It appears that narrating by meaning requires juristic understanding of the Shari'a, and that is not the author's choice, so it should be interpreted that narrating by meaning requires knowledge of the language if the hadith is about linguistic meanings, and juristic understanding of the Shari'a if it is about legal meanings. Yes, the more virtuous is to transmit in its exact form because it is the azima, and it is not a license to drop, and that is apparent. Imam Fakhr al-Islam allowed narrating by meaning except in what is ambiguous (mushtarak), i.e., unclear, whether khafi (hidden), mushkil (difficult), mujmal (summarized), or mutashabih (unclear), unlike the general (amm) and the literal (haqiqa) which are probable for figurative and specification, for it is permissible for the jurist. The detailed classification of this Imam is that the categories are five: That which is transmitted has clear meaning not susceptible to interpretation at all, like the mufassar and muhkam; and that which is susceptible to interpretation but apparent in indication, like the nass and zahir; and that which requires interpretation to act upon, like the mushkil and mushtarak; and that which cannot be grasped by interpretation but requires hearing, like the mujmal; or cannot be grasped at all, like the mutashabih and jawami' al-kalim (comprehensive speech). The first category: narrating by meaning is permissible for anyone who knows the language, because there is no possibility of error in understanding the meaning due to its complete absence of susceptibility to interpretation and specification. As for the second: it is permissible only for a jurist, for a non-jurist may substitute a wording that does not carry that interpretation, and that would be the intended meaning of the Lawgiver, so the ruling would be lost. But the jurist knows the proper meaning of each wording, so he does not change it in a way that turns it from apparent to definitive. As for the third: narrating by meaning is not permissible at all, because the meaning is understood only through interpretation and use of opinion, and the one who opines may err or be correct. So something that is not obligatory to follow becomes obligatory to follow relative to the infallible one. If you say: Perhaps it is known by a circumstantial evidence (qarina)? We say: If the qarina is attached so as to make it clear in language, then it would fall into the first two categories. As for the fourth: narrating by meaning is not possible at all, for the mutashabih is unknown in meaning. As for the mujmal: before hearing the explanation, it is like the mutashabih; after it, the narration is narration of the mujmal and the bayan, and they are two hadiths with clear meaning. As for the fifth: because jawami' al-kalim are a special gift to our Messenger as indicated by the authentic hadith, and the like cannot be produced. So if narrated by meaning, it would be narrated according to one's own understanding and the expression that comes to mind, thereby losing most of the benefits they contain. Then this is his statement on the permissibility of narrating. As for acceptance, there is no dispute; it is accepted absolutely, and it is interpreted that what the narrator transmitted in form is permissible to narrate by meaning because he is upright and does not commit the prohibited or attribute to the Messenger something doubtful. How, if narrated by meaning, the heard wording is not known, so how...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of narrating hadith by meaning (al-naql bi al-ma'na) rather than verbatim. It begins by stating that the determined form (azima) is to transmit the exact wording heard from the Prophet or the teacher, while the concession (rukhsa) is to narrate by meaning for someone who knows the Arabic language and is well-versed in the Shari'a. The author then notes a subtlety: the requirement of 'well-versed in the Shari'a' (mutafaqqih fi al-shari'a) might be for narrating by meaning in general, but a more nuanced view is that it depends on the type of hadith: if the hadith relies on linguistic meanings, knowledge of language suffices; if on legal meanings, then juristic understanding is needed. However, the superior practice is to transmit the exact wording. The passage then presents the detailed classification of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (a Hanafi scholar): He divides hadith texts into five categories regarding the permissibility of narrating by meaning: (1) Texts that are perfectly clear and not open to interpretation (mufassar, muhkam) – anyone who knows Arabic can narrate by meaning. (2) Texts that are apparent in meaning but susceptible to interpretation (nass, zahir) – only a jurist (faqih) may narrate by meaning, because a non-jurist might substitute a wording that changes the intended meaning. (3) Texts that require interpretation to understand (mushkil, mushtarak) – narrating by meaning is not allowed at all because it depends on personal opinion which can be wrong. (4) Texts that are not understood at all (mutashabih) or require hearing explanation (mujmal before explanation) – narrating by meaning is impossible or extremely difficult. (5) 'Jawami' al-kalim' (comprehensive speech) – the Prophet's unique gift of concise speech; its meaning cannot be captured by paraphrase, so narrating by meaning is not allowed as it would lose the full benefit. The passage concludes that acceptance of such narrations (from upright narrators) is unanimous, with the understanding that the narrator is trustworthy and will not misrepresent.

Key Terms
النقل بالمعنى (al-naql bi al-ma'na): Narrating a hadith by conveying its meaning rather than the exact wording.
المفسر (al-mufassar): A text that is explained clearly and is not open to interpretation.
المحكم (al-muhkam): A text that is definitive and cannot be abrogated or changed.
النص (al-nass): A text that is explicit and unambiguous in its indication.
الظاهر (al-zahir): A text that is apparent but susceptible to interpretation.
المشكل (al-mushkil): A text that is difficult to understand and requires effort to interpret.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that has multiple possible meanings.
المجمل (al-mujmal): A text that is summarized and requires explanation (bayan) to be understood.
المتشابه (al-mutashabih): A text whose meaning is unknown or unclear.
جوامع الكلم (jawami' al-kalim): Comprehensive, concise speech; a gift to the Prophet with many meanings in few words.
العزيمة (al-azima): The preferred or determined form of transmission, i.e., exact wording.
الرخصة (al-rukhsa): The concession allowed for those who are knowledgeable, i.e., narrating by meaning.
فخر الإسلام (Fakhr al-Islam): Title of a Hanafi scholar, likely 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari or similar; his classification is cited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Fakhr al-Islam's classification, for which type of hadith text is narrating by meaning absolutely forbidden?
A) Mufassar and Muhkam texts.
B) Nass and Zahir texts.
C) Mushkil and Mushtarak texts.
D) Jawami' al-Kalim.

Key Takeaway
Narrating hadith by meaning is a conditional concession: permissible for clear texts (any Arabic speaker) and apparent texts (only jurists), but forbidden for ambiguous texts that require interpretation or are unique to the Prophet's speech.

Transmission of Hadith by Meaning: Permissibility and Conditions
An usul al-fiqh discussion on the permissibility of transmitting hadith by meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which the Hanafi school permits naql bi al-ma'na and distinguish between categories of hadith texts based on their clarity for transmission.

Core Idea
Transmitting hadith by meaning is a permitted license for qualified scholars, but verbatim transmission remains the ideal.

The transmission of hadith is the backbone of Sunnah. Scholars debated whether a narrator could convey the meaning of a hadith without using the exact words of the Prophet ﷺ. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, which permits naql bi al-ma'na under specific conditions, depending on the textual clarity of the hadith and the narrator's expertise.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between azima and rukhsat
Identify the two methods: verbatim transmission (azima) is the ideal, while transmission by meaning (rukhsat) is a concession.
This distinction is foundational to understanding the Hanafi position.
2
Classify the hadith into one of five categories
The text presents five types of hadith based on their susceptibility to interpretation (ta'wil). Learn each category and its ruling for naql bi al-ma'na.
Categories range from clear texts (mufassar, muhkam) to those requiring external knowledge (mujmal, mutashabih, jawami' al-kalim).
3
Analyze the dispute with prohibitionists
Examine the arguments of those who prohibit naql bi al-ma'na absolutely, and the Hanafi response using Companion practice and the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud.
This step develops critical engagement with differing opinions.
4
Apply the conditions to a case
Imagine a hadith with the wording 'Pray as you have seen me pray.' Determine if it can be transmitted by meaning and by whom.
Apply the classification and condition of the narrator's knowledge.

التعارض كثيراً ( وفتح الباب (التقصير) في حفظ الحديث ( والبدعة) فإن الاعتماد على الخط يؤدي إلى قبول كل مكتوب والتلبيس فيه ممكن بل واقع فينفتح البدعة ألا ترى إلى تحليف) أمير المؤمنين (علي) كيف احتاط هذا (بخلاف العام والحقيقة المحتملين للمجاز والخصوص) فإنه يجوز للفقيه، وتفصيل كلام هذا الإمام أن الأقسام خمسة : أن يكون المنقول متضح المعنى غير قابل للتأويل أصلاً، كالمفسر والمحكم، وما يكون محتملاً للتأويل ظاهراً في الدلالة كالنص والظاهر وما يحتاج فيه إلى التأويل للعمل به كالمشكل والمشترك، وما لا يدرك بالتأويل يحتاج إلى السماع، كالمجمل، أو لا يدرك أصلاً، كالمتشابه وجوامع الكلم، فالأول يجوز نقله بالمعنى الشارع فيفوت الحكم، وأما الفقيه فيعرف حق كل لفظ، فلا يغير بحيث ينقلب من الظهور إلى الأحكام، وأما الثالث : فلا يحل فيه النقل بالمعنى أصلاً، لأن المعنى لا يفهم فيه إلا بتأويل واستعمال رأي والرائي يخطىء ويصيب، فما هو غير واجب الاتباع يصير واجب الاتباع بالنسبة إلى المعصوم صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه فإن قلت: لعله يعرف بالقرينة؟ قلنا : لو كانت القرينة مقرونة بحيث تجعله متضح المعنى لغة فيدخل في الأولين وأما الرابع : فلا يحتمل نقل المعنى فيه فإن المتشابه لا يعرف معناه، وأما المجمل فقبل سماع البيان مثل المتشابه وبعده النقل نقل المجمل والبيان وهما حديثان متضحا المعنى، وأما الخامس فلان جوامع الكلم مخصوص بها إعطاء رسولنا صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كما يدل عليه الخبر الصحيح ولا يمكن إتيان مثله فلو نقل بمعناه نقل على فهمه وعلى ما يتأدى من عبارته فيفوت أكثر الفوائد المشتملة هي عليها، ثم
